Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
8 min
Priority of the Trustee's Claim When a Pledge Is Reclaimed
Ruling on priority when a pledged slave is reclaimed and the trustee pays compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the trustee who pays compensation for a reclaimed pledge has priority over the mortgagee in the sale proceeds, and explain the reasoning based on the debt's connection to the specific slave.

Core Idea
The trustee's claim from paying compensation for a reclaimed pledge is preferred over the mortgagee's general debt.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, we examine a scenario from the law of pledge (rahn). When a pledged slave is reclaimed by a third party, the trustee (عدل) may pay compensation (بدل) to retain possession. The critical question: who has the better right to the sale proceeds—the trustee or the mortgagee? The Hanafi school establishes that the trustee is preferred because his debt arises directly from this particular slave, unlike the mortgagee's debt which is on the debtor generally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Determine who the parties are: the bankrupt debtor (راهن مفلس), the mortgagee (مرتهن), the trustee (عدل), and a third party who reclaims the slave. Understand that the trustee pays compensation and now holds the slave.
2
Recognize the conflict of rights
Both the trustee and the mortgagee have rights to the slave's value: the trustee for his compensation, the mortgagee for his debt. They attach at the same time.
The text says 'تعلق بالعبد في وقت واحد'.
3
Apply the principle of preference
The trustee is given priority because his claim is directly connected to this slave (وجب بسبب هذا العبد), whereas the mortgagee's debt is general.
The text uses an analogy: 'كأنه دين العبد أحق' – like the slave's own debt is more entitled.
4
Contrast with the mortgagee's position
The mortgagee's right to the proceeds only arises after the sale; it does not attach to the slave directly. The trustee's right attached to the slave at the moment he paid compensation.
5
Summarize the ruling
Thus the trustee sells the slave and has priority to recover his compensation; only the remainder, if any, goes to the mortgagee.
Practical outcome of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان الراهن مفلساً والرهن في يد العدل فاستحق العبد فدفع العدل البدل وأبقاه في يده يبيعه ويستوفي ثمنه وهو أحق به من المرتهن لأن حقهما تعلق بالعبد في وقت واحد لأن حق المرتهن فيما تحول من العبد إلى ثمنه بالبيع وإنما يتعلق بالعبد ثانياً بعد الرد وحق العدل تعلق بالعبد في هذا الوقت فقد استوفيا الحقين في وقت تعلق الحق ترجح دين العدل لتعلقه بالعبد وجب بسبب هذا العبد، ودين المرتهن لم يجب بسبب هذا العبد فصار العدل أولى كدين مع دين المولى فيكون دين العبد أحق، وصار كما لو دفع العدل الثمن إلى المرتهن ثم رد عليه بالعيب فيسترد الثمن منه فكذا هذا باع العدل بيعاً فاسداً لا يضمن كالوكيل المفرد. ومعنى الإجبار أن يحبسه القاضي أياماً ليبيع فإن لج بعد الحبس أياماً فالقاضي يبيعه عليه وهذا على أصلهما ظاهر وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة فكذلك عند البعض لأنه تعين جهة لقضاء الدين، ولأن بيع الرهن صار مستحقاً للمرتهن بخلاف سائر المواضع. وقيل: لا يبيع القاضي عنده كما لا يبيع المديون عنده لقضاء الدين ثم إذا أجبر على البيع وباع لا يفسد هذا البيع بهذا الإجبار لأن الإجبار وقع على قضاء الدين بأي طريق شاء حتى لو قضاه بغيره صح وإنما البيع طريق من طرقه، ولأنه إجبار لحق وبمثله لا يكون مكرهاً فلا يفسد إجباره به. ولو لم يكن التوكيل مشروطاً في عقد الرهن وإنما شرطاه بعده قيل لا يجبر لأن التوكيل لم يصر وصفاً من أوصاف الرهن فكانت مفردة كسائر الوكالات وقيل يجبر كي يؤدي حقه وهذا أصح حتى روي عن أبي يوسف أن الجواب في الفصلين واحد في أنه يجبر على القول قضاء، وذكر محمد في الجامع الصغير : والأصل الإجبار مطلقاً من غير تفصيل من تكون الوكالة مشروطة فيه يدل على ذلك، فلو باع العدل خرج من أن يكون رهناً والثمن قائم مقامه فيكون رهناً مكانه وأن يقبضه بعد لقيامه مقام ما كان مقبوضاً بجهة الرهن فإذا توى كان من المرتهن لبقاء عقد الرهن في الثمن لقيامه مقام المبيع المرهون وكذلك إذا قتل العبد الرهن وغرم القاتل قيمته لأن المالك يستحقه من حيث المالية وإن كان بدل الدم فأخذ حكم ضمان المال في حق المستحق فبقي عقد الرهن فيه، وكذلك لو قتله عبده فدفع به لكونه قائماً مقام الأول لحماً ودماً فيكون رهناً مكانه .
If the debtor is bankrupt and the pledge is in the trustee's hand, and the slave (the pledge) is reclaimed (by a third party), and the trustee pays the substitute (badal) and keeps it in his hand to sell it and collect its price, he has a better right to it than the mortgagee, because both their rights attached to the slave at the same time: the mortgagee's right (to the price) transferred from the slave to its price by sale, and it attaches to the slave only secondarily after return, while the trustee's right attached to the slave at this time. So they both have claims at the same time, but the trustee's claim outweighs because it attached to the slave due to this slave, whereas the mortgagee's debt did not arise due to this slave. So the trustee is more entitled, like a debt owed to the slave's owner versus another creditor, so the slave's debt is more entitled. It is as if the trustee paid the price to the mortgagee and then the slave was returned due to a defect, and he takes back the price. Similarly, if the trustee sells with a defective (fasid) sale, he is not liable like a sole agent. The meaning of compulsion (ijbar) is that the judge imprisons him for days to sell; if he persists after days, the judge sells it on his behalf. This is apparent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for Abu Hanifa's principle, some say similarly because the means of debt repayment has been determined, and because the sale of the pledge has become due to the mortgagee unlike other cases. Some say: the judge does not sell according to him just as he does not sell for a debtor to pay a debt. Then, when he is compelled to sell and sells, this sale is not rendered invalid by the compulsion because the compulsion was to discharge the debt by any means, even if he discharged it by other means it would be valid, and sale is just one means; and because it is compulsion for a right, such compulsion does not make him coerced (mukrah) in a voiding sense. And if the appointment of the agent was not stipulated in the contract of pledge but was stipulated afterwards, some say he is not compelled to sell because the agency did not become a description of the pledge, so it is like ordinary agencies. Some say he is compelled to fulfill the right, and this is more correct, until it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the answer in both cases (stipulated at the time of contract or later) is the same: he is compelled to the recommended opinion (qawl qada). Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: the principle is compulsion absolutely without distinction of whether the agency was stipulated. That indicates. So if the trustee sells, the (pledged item) ceases to be a pledge and the price stands in its place, becoming a pledge in its stead, and it must be received thereafter because it stands in the place of what was received by virtue of the pledge. If it perishes, it is from the mortgagee because the contract of pledge remains in the price as it stands in place of the sold pledged item. Similarly, if the slave (the pledge) is killed and the killer pays its value, the owner receives it from the financial perspective even if it is blood money, so it takes the ruling of guarantee of property regarding the claimant, and the pledge contract remains in it. Similarly, if his slave kills him and he is handed over as blood money because he stands in place of the first in flesh and blood, so he becomes a pledge in its place.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This is a complex ruling combining several scenarios. First, if the debtor is bankrupt and a third party reclaims the pledged slave (proving it was his property), the trustee may pay compensation (بدل) to the claimant and retain the slave to sell and recover the compensation. In this situation, the trustee has a prior right over the mortgagee (creditor) to the proceeds, because the trustee's claim arose from this particular slave (he paid the price for it), while the mortgagee's debt is general. This is akin to a debt of the slave (e.g., for its maintenance) taking precedence over a mortgage. The passage then discusses the judge's power to compel the trustee to sell the pledge if he refuses. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the judge can imprison him to force sale. For Abu Hanifa, some hold the same view because the trustee's duty has become specific; others say the judge does not directly sell (as he would not for an ordinary debtor). If the trustee is compelled to sell, the sale is valid because compulsion for a legitimate right does not invalidate consent. If the agency was not stipulated in the original pledge contract but later agreed, there is a dispute: some say the judge cannot compel; others (preferred) say he can, and Abu Yusuf reportedly agreed that compulsion is the same in both cases. Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir supports absolute compulsion. Finally, the text explains that upon a valid sale by the trustee, the price substitutes for the pledged item and becomes a new pledge ('رهناً مكانه'). If the price perishes, it is from the mortgagee's risk because the pledge contract continues in the price. Similarly, if the pledged slave is killed, the blood money (قيمة) stands in place of the slave as pledge; even if the killer is a slave, the slave handed over as compensation becomes a pledge.

Key Terms
البدل (al-badal): Compensation paid by the trustee to the reclaiming owner of the slave; the trustee then holds the slave as security for that compensation.
الإجبار (al-ijbār): Legal compulsion by the judge to force a party to fulfill an obligation, here to sell the pledge.
القيمة (al-qīmah): Value or blood money paid when the pledged slave is killed; stands as substitute pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the trustee have priority over the mortgagee in the sale proceeds of a reclaimed pledge for which he paid compensation?
A) Because the trustee is a state-appointed official.
B) Because the mortgagee's debt is general, while the trustee's claim is directly due to this specific slave.
C) Because the trustee is a co-owner of the slave.
D) Because the mortgagee waived his right.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a trustee pays compensation for a reclaimed pledge, his claim to the sale proceeds takes priority over the mortgagee's claim because his debt arises directly from that specific slave.

Compulsion (Ijbar) to Sell the Pledge: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on the judge's power to compel sale of the pledge by the trustee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding the judge's power to compel the trustee to sell a pledge, and apply the relied-upon ruling of absolute compulsion.

Core Idea
The judge can compel the trustee to sell the pledge, and such compulsion does not invalidate the sale.

This part of the passage addresses legal compulsion (إجبار) to sell the pledge when the trustee refuses. The Hanafi school discusses whether the judge can imprison and eventually sell on his behalf. There is an intra-madhhab difference: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, compulsion is allowed; according to Abu Hanifa, some say it is not. The passage resolves in favor of compulsion, supported by Muhammad's statement in al-Jami' al-Saghir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario for compulsion
The trustee refuses to sell the pledge. The judge may imprison him and, after a few days, sell the pledge on his behalf if he persists.
The text says 'يحبسه القاضي أياماً ليبيع فإن لج بعد الحبس أياماً فالقاضي يبيعه عليه'.
2
Note the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
They hold that compulsion is allowed (ظاهر على أصلهما). The judge can sell directly.
For them, the compulsion is clear.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's position
According to some, Abu Hanifa agrees because the means of debt repayment is determined; according to others, he does not allow compulsion, similar to an ordinary debtor.
The text says 'وقيل: لا يبيع القاضي عنده كما لا يبيع المديون عنده لقضاء الدين'.
4
Resolve the ikhtilaf
The passage favors compulsion. Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir states the principle is absolute compulsion (الإجبار مطلقاً). Abu Yusuf also reportedly gave the same answer for both cases (agency stipulated or not).
Thus the mufta bihi is that compulsion is valid.
5
Understand the effect of compulsion on the sale's validity
Even if the trustee is compelled, the sale is valid because the compulsion is for a legitimate right and does not make him coerced (مكرهاً) in a voiding sense.
The text: 'لا يفسد هذا البيع بهذا الإجبار لأن الإجبار وقع على قضاء الدين... و لأنه إجبار لحق و بمثله لا يكون مكرهاً'.

Source Text

الإجبار (al-ijbār): Legal compulsion by the judge to force a party to fulfill an obligation, here to sell the pledge.
التوكيل (al-tawkīl): Appointment of an agent (trustee) to sell the pledge.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the effect of judicial compulsion on the validity of a sale of pledge?
A) The sale is invalid because the seller was coerced.
B) The sale is valid because the compulsion was for a legitimate right.
C) The sale is valid only if the trustee consents after imprisonment.
D) The sale is suspended pending the debtor's approval.

Key Takeaway
The judge can compel the trustee to sell a pledge, and the sale remains valid because compulsion for a legitimate right does not constitute unlawful coercion.

Substitution of Price and Compensation as New Pledge
Ruling on substitution of price and compensation as new pledge after sale or destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that when a pledge is sold or destroyed, its price or compensation (diya/qimah) substitutes as a new pledge, and identify the consequences for the mortgagee's security.

Core Idea
Sale or destruction of a pledge does not end the security; its price or compensation becomes the new pledge.

The final part of the passage discusses what happens after the trustee sells the pledge or the pledge is destroyed. The key principle is that the pledge contract continues in the price or compensation (badal/qimah). This ensures that the mortgagee's security is not lost. The text also covers scenarios where the pledged slave is killed and the killer pays blood money, or even when the killer is a slave who is handed over as compensation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the general rule of substitution
When the trustee sells the pledge, the pledge ceases to be a pledge, but the price stands in its place as a new pledge.
Text: 'خرج من أن يكون رهناً والثمن قائم مقامه فيكون رهناً مكانه'.
2
Understand the risk of perishing
If the price perishes after being received by the mortgagee, the loss is from the mortgagee because the pledge contract remains in the price.
Text: 'إذا توى كان من المرتهن لبقاء عقد الرهن في الثمن'.
3
Apply substitution to compensation for destruction
If the pledged slave is killed and the killer pays its value (qimah), that value takes the place of the slave as a pledge.
Text: 'إذا قتل العبد الرهن وغرم القاتل قيمته... بقي عقد الرهن فيه'.
4
Extend to case of a slave killer handed over
If the killer is a slave and is handed over as blood money, that slave becomes the new pledge.
Text: 'لو قتله عبده فدفع به... فيكون رهناً مكانه'.
5
Summarize the effect on the mortgagee's security
In all cases, the mortgagee's security is preserved through substitution, whether in price, value, or a substitute slave.
The contract of pledge is not defeated by sale or destruction.

Source Text

ولو كان الراهن مفلساً والرهن في يد العدل فاستحق العبد فدفع العدل البدل وأبقاه في يده يبيعه ويستوفي ثمنه وهو أحق به من المرتهن لأن حقهما تعلق بالعبد في وقت واحد لأن حق المرتهن فيما تحول من العبد إلى ثمنه بالبيع وإنما يتعلق بالعبد ثانياً بعد الرد وحق العدل تعلق بالعبد في هذا الوقت فقد استوفيا الحقين في وقت تعلق الحق ترجح دين العدل لتعلقه بالعبد وجب بسبب هذا العبد، ودين المرتهن لم يجب بسبب هذا العبد فصار العدل أولى كدين مع دين المولى فيكون دين العبد أحق، وصار كما لو دفع العدل الثمن إلى المرتهن ثم رد عليه بالعيب فيسترد الثمن منه فكذا هذا باع العدل بيعاً فاسداً لا يضمن كالوكيل المفرد. ومعنى الإجبار أن يحبسه القاضي أياماً ليبيع فإن لج بعد الحبس أياماً فالقاضي يبيعه عليه وهذا على أصلهما ظاهر وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة فكذلك عند البعض لأنه تعين جهة لقضاء الدين، ولأن بيع الرهن صار مستحقاً للمرتهن بخلاف سائر المواضع. وقيل: لا يبيع القاضي عنده كما لا يبيع المديون عنده لقضاء الدين ثم إذا أجبر على البيع وباع لا يفسد هذا البيع بهذا الإجبار لأن الإجبار وقع على قضاء الدين بأي طريق شاء حتى لو قضاه بغيره صح وإنما البيع طريق من طرقه، ولأنه إجبار لحق وبمثله لا يكون مكرهاً فلا يفسد إجباره به. ولو لم يكن التوكيل مشروطاً في عقد الرهن وإنما شرطاه بعده قيل لا يجبر لأن التوكيل لم يصر وصفاً من أوصاف الرهن فكانت مفردة كسائر الوكالات وقيل يجبر كي يؤدي حقه وهذا أصح حتى روي عن أبي يوسف أن الجواب في الفصلين واحد في أنه يجبر على القول قضاء، وذكر محمد في الجامع الصغير : والأصل الإجبار مطلقاً من غير تفصيل من تكون الوكالة مشروطة فيه يدل على ذلك، فلو باع العدل خرج من أن يكون رهناً والثمن قائم مقامه فيكون رهناً مكانه وأن يقبضه بعد لقيامه مقام ما كان مقبوضاً بجهة الرهن فإذا توى كان من المرتهن لبقاء عقد الرهن في الثمن لقيامه مقام المبيع المرهون وكذلك إذا قتل العبد الرهن وغرم القاتل قيمته لأن المالك يستحقه من حيث المالية وإن كان بدل الدم فأخذ حكم ضمان المال في حق المستحق فبقي عقد الرهن فيه، وكذلك لو قتله عبده فدفع به لكونه قائماً مقام الأول لحماً ودماً فيكون رهناً مكانه .
If the debtor is bankrupt and the pledge is in the trustee's hand, and the slave (the pledge) is reclaimed (by a third party), and the trustee pays the substitute (badal) and keeps it in his hand to sell it and collect its price, he has a better right to it than the mortgagee, because both their rights attached to the slave at the same time: the mortgagee's right (to the price) transferred from the slave to its price by sale, and it attaches to the slave only secondarily after return, while the trustee's right attached to the slave at this time. So they both have claims at the same time, but the trustee's claim outweighs because it attached to the slave due to this slave, whereas the mortgagee's debt did not arise due to this slave. So the trustee is more entitled, like a debt owed to the slave's owner versus another creditor, so the slave's debt is more entitled. It is as if the trustee paid the price to the mortgagee and then the slave was returned due to a defect, and he takes back the price. Similarly, if the trustee sells with a defective (fasid) sale, he is not liable like a sole agent. The meaning of compulsion (ijbar) is that the judge imprisons him for days to sell; if he persists after days, the judge sells it on his behalf. This is apparent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for Abu Hanifa's principle, some say similarly because the means of debt repayment has been determined, and because the sale of the pledge has become due to the mortgagee unlike other cases. Some say: the judge does not sell according to him just as he does not sell for a debtor to pay a debt. Then, when he is compelled to sell and sells, this sale is not rendered invalid by the compulsion because the compulsion was to discharge the debt by any means, even if he discharged it by other means it would be valid, and sale is just one means; and because it is compulsion for a right, such compulsion does not make him coerced (mukrah) in a voiding sense. And if the appointment of the agent was not stipulated in the contract of pledge but was stipulated afterwards, some say he is not compelled to sell because the agency did not become a description of the pledge, so it is like ordinary agencies. Some say he is compelled to fulfill the right, and this is more correct, until it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the answer in both cases (stipulated at the time of contract or later) is the same: he is compelled to the recommended opinion (qawl qada). Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: the principle is compulsion absolutely without distinction of whether the agency was stipulated. That indicates. So if the trustee sells, the (pledged item) ceases to be a pledge and the price stands in its place, becoming a pledge in its stead, and it must be received thereafter because it stands in the place of what was received by virtue of the pledge. If it perishes, it is from the mortgagee because the contract of pledge remains in the price as it stands in place of the sold pledged item. Similarly, if the slave (the pledge) is killed and the killer pays its value, the owner receives it from the financial perspective even if it is blood money, so it takes the ruling of guarantee of property regarding the claimant, and the pledge contract remains in it. Similarly, if his slave kills him and he is handed over as blood money because he stands in place of the first in flesh and blood, so he becomes a pledge in its place.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This is a complex ruling combining several scenarios. First, if the debtor is bankrupt and a third party reclaims the pledged slave (proving it was his property), the trustee may pay compensation (بدل) to the claimant and retain the slave to sell and recover the compensation. In this situation, the trustee has a prior right over the mortgagee (creditor) to the proceeds, because the trustee's claim arose from this particular slave (he paid the price for it), while the mortgagee's debt is general. This is akin to a debt of the slave (e.g., for its maintenance) taking precedence over a mortgage. The passage then discusses the judge's power to compel the trustee to sell the pledge if he refuses. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the judge can imprison him to force sale. For Abu Hanifa, some hold the same view because the trustee's duty has become specific; others say the judge does not directly sell (as he would not for an ordinary debtor). If the trustee is compelled to sell, the sale is valid because compulsion for a legitimate right does not invalidate consent. If the agency was not stipulated in the original pledge contract but later agreed, there is a dispute: some say the judge cannot compel; others (preferred) say he can, and Abu Yusuf reportedly agreed that compulsion is the same in both cases. Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir supports absolute compulsion. Finally, the text explains that upon a valid sale by the trustee, the price substitutes for the pledged item and becomes a new pledge ('رهناً مكانه'). If the price perishes, it is from the mortgagee's risk because the pledge contract continues in the price. Similarly, if the pledged slave is killed, the blood money (قيمة) stands in place of the slave as pledge; even if the killer is a slave, the slave handed over as compensation becomes a pledge.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): Price received from sale of the pledge; stands as substitute pledge.
القيمة (al-qīmah): Value or blood money paid when the pledged slave is killed; stands as substitute pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the price when the trustee sells the pledged item?
A) The price is returned to the debtor.
B) The price becomes a new pledge securing the mortgagee's debt.
C) The price is divided equally between mortgagee and debtor.
D) The price is held by the trustee until the mortgagee demands it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the pledge contract is not terminated by sale or destruction; the price or compensation substitutes as the new security.

Defective Sale and Judicial Compulsion in Pledge Transactions
Ruling on defective sale and judicial compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a defective sale by a trustee and explain the judge's authority to compel a sale, identifying the intra-Hanafi dispute on compulsion.

Core Idea
A trustee is not liable for a defective sale; the judge can compel a sale for a right without making it void.

In pledge transactions, the trustee ('adl) is a trusted intermediary. This lesson examines two issues: the trustee's liability when he sells the pledge in a defective manner, and the judge's power to compel a recalcitrant trustee to sell. The Hanafi school navigates these with nuanced reasoning, distinguishing between types of compulsion and the scope of the trustee's agency. By the end, you will appreciate how the school balances the creditor's right with the trustee's fiduciary role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on defective sale
Note the explicit statement: the trustee is not liable for a defective sale, just like a sole agent. Understand the legal principle behind this.
This is a clear ruling; the defect does not create liability.
2
Define judicial compulsion (ijbār)
The passage explains that ijbār means the judge imprisons the trustee to force a sale, and if he persists, the judge sells on his behalf.
Compulsion here is for a right, not an arbitrary act.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on compulsion; for Abu Hanifa there are two views. Distinguish the positions.
The dispute centers on whether the pledge gives the mortgagee a right to demand sale.
4
Evaluate the effect of compulsion on sale validity
Even under compulsion, the sale is valid because the compulsion is to discharge a debt, not to sell per se, and it is for a legitimate right.
This distinguishes it from duress (ikrah) that invalidates contracts.
5
Examine the condition of stipulated agency
If the agency was not originally stipulated but added later, the more correct view is that compulsion still applies. This is based on Imam Muhammad's general principle.
The school's preferred position is absolute compulsion when a right exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باع العدل بيعاً فاسداً لا يضمن كالوكيل المفرد. ومعنى الإجبار أن يحبسه القاضي أياماً ليبيع فإن لج بعد الحبس أياماً فالقاضي يبيعه عليه وهذا على أصلهما ظاهر وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة فكذلك عند البعض لأنه تعين جهة لقضاء الدين، ولأن بيع الرهن صار مستحقاً للمرتهن بخلاف سائر المواضع. وقيل: لا يبيع القاضي عنده كما لا يبيع المديون عنده لقضاء الدين ثم إذا أجبر على البيع وباع لا يفسد هذا البيع بهذا الإجبار لأن الإجبار وقع على قضاء الدين بأي طريق شاء حتى لو قضاه بغيره صح وإنما البيع طريق من طرقه، ولأنه إجبار لحق وبمثله لا يكون مكرهاً فلا يفسد إجباره به. ولو لم يكن التوكيل مشروطاً في عقد الرهن وإنما شرطاه بعده قيل لا يجبر لأن التوكيل لم يصر وصفاً من أوصاف الرهن فكانت مفردة كسائر الوكالات وقيل يجبر كي يؤدي حقه وهذا أصح حتى روي عن أبي يوسف أن الجواب في الفصلين واحد في أنه يجبر على القول قضاء، وذكر محمد في الجامع الصغير : والأصل الإجبار مطلقاً من غير تفصيل من تكون الوكالة مشروطة فيه يدل على ذلك.
If the trustee sells with a defective (fasid) sale, he is not liable just like the sole agent. The meaning of compulsion (ijbar) is that the judge imprisons him for days to sell; if he persists after days, the judge sells it on his behalf. This is apparent according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for Abu Hanifa's principle, some say similarly because the means of debt repayment has been determined, and because the sale of the pledge has become due to the mortgagee unlike other cases. Some say: the judge does not sell according to him just as he does not sell for a debtor to pay a debt. Then, when he is compelled to sell and sells, this sale is not rendered invalid by the compulsion because the compulsion was to discharge the debt by any means, even if he discharged it by other means it would be valid, and sale is just one means; and because it is compulsion for a right, such compulsion does not make him coerced (mukrah) in a voiding sense. And if the appointment of the agent was not stipulated in the contract of pledge but was stipulated afterwards, some say he is not compelled because the agency did not become a description of the pledge, so it is like ordinary agencies. Some say he is compelled to fulfill the right, and this is more correct, until it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the answer in both cases (whether stipulated at the time of contract or later) is the same: he is compelled to the recommended opinion (qawl qada). Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: the principle is compulsion absolutely without distinction of whether the agency was stipulated. That indicates.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: the trustee's liability for a defective sale, and the judge's power to compel a sale. First, if the trustee sells the pledge in a defective manner (fasid), he is not liable for any loss because he acted as an agent (like a sole sale agent) and did not exceed his authority. The defective sale can be rectified or the item returned. Second, if the trustee refuses to sell, the judge may compel him by imprisonment. This is clear according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions). Regarding Abu Hanifa, there are two views: some say the judge can compel because the sale has become obligatory for the mortgagee; others say he cannot because Abu Hanifa generally does not compel a debtor to sell property to pay a debt. In either case, if the trustee ultimately sells under compulsion, the sale is valid because the compulsion is for a legitimate right and does not constitute duress that invalidates consent. The text then discusses whether the compulsion applies if the agency was not originally stipulated in the pledge contract but added later. Some say no, because it is then an independent agency; others say yes, and this is more correct (أصح). Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir supports the view of absolute compulsion.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay‘ al-fāsid): A sale with a defect that makes it invalid in essence but can be rectified; the trustee is not liable for it.
الإجبار (al-ijbār): Compulsion by the judge to force the trustee to sell the pledge.
الوكالة المشروطة (al-wikālah al-mashrūṭah): Agency that is stipulated as a condition in the original pledge contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because he mismanaged the sale.
B) No, because he is like a sole agent and is not liable.
C) Only if the sale causes loss.
D) It depends on whether the sale was ratified.

Key Takeaway
The trustee is not liable for a defective sale, and the judge can compel a sale for a legitimate right without invalidating it.

Liability of the Trustee When the Sold Pawn is Claimed
Ruling on trustee liability with comparative debt priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of the trustee when the pawn is claimed and apply the Hanafi rule of priority of debts attached to the pawn.

Core Idea
The trustee's debt attaches to the pawn itself, so it takes priority over the mortgagee's debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a nuanced scenario in pawn (rahn). When a trustee sells the pawn and pays the mortgagee, but the pawn is later claimed by a rightful owner, the trustee becomes liable. The question is: whom must he compensate? The ruling hinges on the priority of debts attached to the pawn, analogous to a slave's debt versus his master's debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the parties: pawnor (rahin), mortgagee (murtahin), trustee ('adl). The trustee sells the pawn and pays the mortgagee. Then the pawn is claimed (istiḥqāq) by a third party. The trustee is liable (ḍāmin).
2
State the ruling
The text states: 'Fal-'adlu yaḍmanu al-rāhina qīmatahu aw al-murtahina thamanahu' – the trustee must guarantee the pawnor its value OR the mortgagee its price. He has a choice.
3
Trace the proof (dalil)
The reasoning (ta'līl) compares the trustee's debt (for his fee or guarantee) and the mortgagee's debt (the loan). The trustee's debt is attached directly to the pawn (slave) because it became obligatory due to this pawn. The mortgagee's debt is not attached to this specific pawn but to the price received. Therefore, the trustee's right is superior.
The analogy is made to the case where a slave's debt competes with his master's debt: the slave's debt is paid first.
4
Apply the qiyas
The passage analogizes to two cases: (1) slave's debt vs. master's debt – slave's debt has priority; (2) if the trustee paid the price and the pawn is returned due to a defect, he recovers the price from the mortgagee. This supports the ruling.
5
Note the exception
If the sale was defective (bā'i‘ fāsid), the trustee is not liable, like an independent agent (wakīl mufrad). This is because the defective sale does not transfer ownership properly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن باب الرهن يوضع على يد عدل وأوف مرتهنه ثمنه فاستحق الرهن وضمن فالعدل يضمن الراهن قيمته أو المرتهن ثمنه لأنه العبد ويستوفي ثمنه وهو أحق به من المرتهن لأن حقهما تعلق بالعبد في وقت واحد لأن حق المرتهن فيما تحول من العبد إلى ثمنه بالبيع وإنما يتعلق بالعبد ثانياً بعد الرد وحق العدل تعلق بالعبد في هذا الوقت فقد استوفيا الحقين في وقت تعلق الحق ترجح دين العدل لتعلقه بالعبد وجب بسبب هذا العبد، ودين المرتهن لم يجب بسبب هذا العبد فصار العدل أولى كدين مع دين المولى فيكون دين العبد أحق، وصار كما لو دفع العدل الثمن إلى المرتهن ثم رد عليه بالعيب فيسترد الثمن منه فكذا هذا باع العدل بيعاً فاسداً لا يضمن كالوكيل المفرد.
Book of Pledge, Chapter on the pledge placed with a trustee: And he gave his mortgagee its price, then the pawn was claimed and he (the trustee) was liable, so the trustee must guarantee the pawner its value or the mortgagee its price. Because the slave (the pawn) and he recovers its price, and he has more right to it than the mortgagee, because their rights attached to the slave at one time. Because the right of the mortgagee is in what transferred from the slave to its price by sale, and it only attaches to the slave secondarily after return, whereas the right of the trustee attached to the slave at this time. So both have received their rights at the time of attachment, the debt of the trustee is preferred because it attached to the slave, it became obligatory because of this slave, whereas the debt of the mortgagee did not become obligatory because of this slave. So the trustee is more entitled, like a debt with a master's debt, so the slave's debt is more entitled. And it is as if the trustee paid the price to the mortgagee then the slave was returned due to a defect, so he recovers the price from him. Similarly, if the trustee made a defective sale, he is not liable, like an independent agent.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It addresses a scenario where the trustee ('adl) sells the pawned item (a slave), pays the mortgagee (murtahin) the price, then the slave is claimed by a third party who proves ownership (mustahaqq). The trustee is now liable (ḍāmin). The question is: whom must the trustee compensate? The ruling: he must compensate either the pawner (rahin) by paying the value of the slave, or the mortgagee by paying the price he received. The reasoning compares two debts: the trustee's right (for his fee or guarantee) is attached directly to the slave, while the mortgagee's debt (the loan) is not attached to this specific slave but to its price. Since the trustee's debt arose from the slave itself, it has priority over the mortgagee's debt, which originated from the loan (unrelated to the slave). This is analogized to the case where a slave's own debt competes with his master's debt: the slave's debt is paid first. Similarly, if the trustee had made a defective sale, he would not be liable, just as an independent agent (wakīl mufrad) is not liable for a flawed sale.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): Trustee; a reliable person appointed to hold the pawned item.
مرتهن (murtahin): Mortgagee; the creditor who receives the pawn as security.
راهن (rahin): Pawnor; the debtor who gives the pawn.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Claim; a third party proves ownership of the pawned item.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability/guarantee; obligation to compensate for loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the trustee's liability when the pawn is claimed after sale?
A) He is not liable because he acted with permission.
B) He must compensate the pawnor only.
C) He must compensate either the pawnor (value) or the mortgagee (price).
D) He must compensate both the pawnor and the mortgagee.

Key Takeaway
The trustee's liability is to compensate either the pawnor or the mortgagee because his debt attaches directly to the pawn, giving it priority.

Compelling the Trustee to Sell and Substitution of the Pawn
Discusses the ruling on forcing the trustee to sell the pawn and the role of authorization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compelling the trustee to sell a pawn and explain how the sale proceeds or compensation take the place of the pawn.

Core Idea
The trustee can be compelled to sell the pawn; the sale is valid and the price or compensation becomes the new pawn.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical dilemma: what happens when the trustee (the neutral custodian of a pawn) refuses to sell it to repay the mortgagee? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, but the relied-upon position allows the judge to compel the trustee—even if authorization to sell was not stipulated in the original contract. Moreover, the passage clarifies that once sold, the price stands in place of the pawn as a new pawn, and similar substitution occurs when the pawn is killed and compensation is paid. Understanding these rules ensures the mortgagee's right is protected without invalidating the transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on compulsion
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow the judge to imprison the trustee to force a sale, while a narration from Abu Hanifa suggests no compulsion. Recognize the tarjih (preferred view) in favour of compulsion.
The debate hinges on whether the pawn is a specific means of repayment.
2
Validate the sale after compulsion
Understand that even if the trustee sells under compulsion, the sale is valid. The compulsion is directed at repaying the debt, not at the sale itself.
The trustee may repay from other sources; the sale is just one route.
3
Analyse the effect of authorization (tawkīl)
Distinguish between authorization stipulated in the original contract and authorization added later. Note the two views and the preferred ruling: compulsion applies in both cases.
Evidence from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad’s statement in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
4
Trace the substitution of the price
After sale, the price becomes the new pawn, and the pledge contract continues on it. The trustee must take possession of the price.
Loss of the price is borne by the mortgagee due to the ongoing contract.
5
Apply the analogy to compensation for killed pawn
If a pawned slave is killed and the killer pays compensation, that compensation becomes the new pawn. Similarly, if the pawnor’s slave kills the pawn, the killer slave becomes the pawn.
The substitution maintains the pledge relationship.

ومعنى الإجبار أن يحبسه القاضي أياماً ليبيع فإن لج بعد الحبس أياماً فالقاضي يبيعه عليه وهذا على أصلهما ظاهر وأما على أصل أبي حنيفة فكذلك عند البعض لأنه تعين جهة لقضاء الدين، ولأن بيع الرهن صار مستحقاً للمرتهن بخلاف سائر المواضع. وقيل: لا يبيع القاضي عنده كما لا يبيع المديون عنده لقضاء الدين ثم إذا أجبر على البيع وباع لا يفسد هذا البيع بهذا الإجبار لأن الإجبار وقع على قضاء الدين بأي طريق شاء حتى لو قضاه بغيره صح وإنما البيع طريق من طرقه، ولأنه إجبار لحق وبمثله لا يكون مكرهاً فلا يفسد إجباره به. ولو لم يكن التوكيل مشروطاً في عقد الرهن وإنما شرطاه بعده قيل لا يجبر لأن التوكيل لم يصر وصفاً من أوصاف الرهن فكانت مفردة كسائر الوكالات وقيل يجبر كي يؤدي حقه وهذا أصح حتى روي عن أبي يوسف أن الجواب في الفصلين واحد في أنه يجبر على القول قضاء، وذكر محمد في الجامع الصغير : والأصل الإجبار مطلقاً من غير تفصيل من تكون الوكالة مشروطة فيه يدل على ذلك، فلو باع العدل خرج من أن يكون رهناً والثمن قائم مقامه فيكون رهناً مكانه وأن يقبضه بعد لقيامه مقام ما كان مقبوضاً بجهة الرهن فإذا توى كان من المرتهن لبقاء عقد الرهن في الثمن لقيامه مقام المبيع المرهون وكذلك إذا قتل العبد الرهن وغرم القاتل قيمته لأن المالك يستحقه من حيث المالية وإن كان بدل الدم فأخذ حكم ضمان المال في حق المستحق فبقي عقد الرهن فيه، وكذلك لو قتله عبده فدفع به لكونه قائماً مقام الأول لحماً ودماً فيكون رهناً مكانه.
And the meaning of compulsion is that the judge imprisons him for days to sell. If he persists after imprisonment for days, then the judge sells it over him. This is apparent according to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) principle. As for according to Abu Hanifa's principle, likewise according to some, because the means of repaying the debt became specific, and because the sale of the pawn became a right of the mortgagee unlike other cases. And it is said: The judge does not sell according to him (Abu Hanifa), just as he does not sell for the debtor to repay a debt. Then if he is compelled to sell and he sells, this sale is not invalidated by this compulsion, because the compulsion was for repaying the debt in any manner he wishes, even if he repays it otherwise it is valid; sale is merely one of the ways. And because it is compulsion for a right, and with such he is not coerced, so his compulsion does not invalidate. And if the authorization was not stipulated in the contract of pledge but they stipulated it afterwards, it is said he is not compelled, because the authorization did not become an attribute of the pledge, so it is separate like other agencies. And it is said he is compelled to fulfill his right, and this is more correct, until it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that the answer in both cases is one: he is compelled according to the saying of Qada. And Muhammad mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: The principle is compulsion absolutely without detail; whoever has authorization stipulated in it indicates that. So if the trustee sells, the pawn ceases to be pawn and the price stands in its place, so it becomes pawn in its place, and he must take possession of it afterwards because it stands in place of what was possessed by virtue of the pledge. So if it is lost, it is from the mortgagee because the pledge contract remains in the price, since it stands in place of the sold pawn. Similarly, if the slave pawn is killed and the killer pays his value, because the owner is entitled to it from the aspect of property even if it is blood money, so it takes the ruling of financial guarantee in the right of the claimant, so the pledge contract remains in it. Similarly, if his slave kills him and he pays by him, because he stands in place of the first in flesh and blood, so he becomes pawn in his place.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage discusses the issue of forcing (ijbār) the trustee ('adl) to sell the pawn when he refuses. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the judge can imprison the trustee to compel him to sell, and if he persists, the judge sells the pawn on his behalf. According to Abu Hanifa, there is a difference of opinion: some say the judge can also compel, because the means of debt repayment is specified (the pawn), and the mortgagee has a right to sell. Others say the judge does not compel, just as he does not force a debtor to sell his property to repay a debt. However, if the trustee sells after being compelled, the sale is valid because the compulsion was for the right of repayment, not for the sale itself. The passage then addresses whether the trustee can be compelled if the authorization to sell (tawkīl) was not stipulated in the original pledge contract but added later. Two views are given: one says no, because the authorization is separate from the pledge; the other says yes, and this is considered more correct (aṣaḥ), supported by a narration from Abu Yusuf. Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Jami' al-Saghir states the principle of compulsion absolutely, without distinction. This view is preferred by later scholars. The passage then explains that when the trustee sells, the price takes the place of the pawn as a new pawn, and the pledge contract continues in it. It also gives analogies: if the pawned slave is killed and the killer pays compensation, that compensation becomes the new pawn; similarly, if the pawnor's slave kills the pawn, the killer slave becomes the pawn.

Key Terms
إجبار (ijbār): Compulsion by the judge to perform an act, such as selling the pawn.
توكيل (tawkīl): Authorization given by the pawnor to the trustee to sell the pawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can the trustee be compelled to sell the pawn if the authorization was not stipulated in the original pledge contract?
A) No, because the authorization is separate
B) Yes, because the authorization becomes part of the pawn attributes
C) Only if the mortgagee requests
D) Only if the pawnor agrees

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows the judge to compel the trustee to sell a pawn, and the sale proceeds or any compensation for the pawn become the new pawn, ensuring the creditor's right is secured.

Trustee Liability and Recourse When a Sold Pawn Is Claimed
Expansion of trustee liability with case analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the trustee's liability and recourse options when a sold pawn is claimed, distinguishing between the two scenarios of the pawn being destroyed or still in existence.

Core Idea
When a sold pawn is claimed, the trustee's liability and right of recourse depend on whether the pawn was destroyed or still exists, and on the claimant's choice.

This lesson unpacks the ruling from the matn on the trustee's liability when a pawned item sold by the trustee is later claimed by a rightful owner. The text examines two main contingencies: the pawn is destroyed or still in existence. For each, it traces the options of the claimant, the trustee, and the buyer, and clarifies the trustee's subsequent recourse against the pawnor or mortgagee. The discussion also covers the effect of the authorization given to the trustee in the original pledge contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Cases
The ruling begins by splitting the scenario into two: the pawn is destroyed (hālik) or still in existence (qā'im). This is the key distinction that determines the options available to the claimant and the trustee.
The analysis proceeds from the fact that the pawn has been sold by the trustee and the mortgagee has been paid.
2
Analyze the Destroyed-Pawn Case
When the pawn is destroyed, the claimant (mustahaqq) may claim compensation either from the pawnor (rahin) or from the trustee ('adl). Trace each path: If from the pawnor, the sale is effective and the mortgagee's debt is satisfied. If from the trustee, the trustee pays and then has the option to recourse against the pawnor (as agent) or against the mortgagee (to recover the price).
The pawnor is considered a usurper (ghāṣib) in relation to the claimant; the trustee is also a usurper by selling and delivering.
3
Analyze the Existing-Pawn Case
When the pawn still exists, the claimant takes it directly from the buyer. The buyer then recourses against the trustee for the price. The Trustee, after paying the buyer, has the same two options: recourse against the pawnor or against the mortgagee.
In this case, the buyer's right to recourse is based on the contract; the trustee is the contracting party.
4
Distinguish Authorization Types
The text concludes with a discussion on whether the authorization (tawkīl) to sell was stipulated in the pledge contract or not. If not stipulated, some scholars hold the trustee is not compelled to sell, while the stronger view (according to Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam) treats it as equal, making the trustee's recourse rights the same in both cases.
This affects the trustee's ability to recourse against the mortgagee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال مال رحمه الله : (وإن باعه العدل وأوف مرتهنه ثمنه فاستحق الرهن وضمن فالعدل يضمن الراهن قيمته أو المرتهن ثمنه وكشف هذا أن المرهون المبيع إذا استحق إما أن يكون قائماً أو هالكاً، ففي الوجه الثاني المستحق بالخيار إن شاء ضمن الراهن لأنه غاصب في حقه بالأخذ أو التسليم وإن شاء ضمن العدل لأنه متعد مثله بالبيع والتسليم فصار غاصباً بذلك، فإذا ضمن الرهن نفذ البيع وصح الاقتضاء لأن الراهن قد تملكه بأداء الضمان مستنداً إلى وقت الغصب فتبين أنه أمره ببيع ملك نفسه، وإن ضمن المستحق العدل نفذ البيع أيضاً لأن العدل ملكه بأداء الضمان ثم هو بالخيار إن شاء رجع على الراهن بالقيمة لأنه وكيل من جهته عامل له فيه فيرجع عليه بما لحقه من العهدة بالغرر من جهته ونفذ البيع لأن الرهن لما كان مدار الضمان عليه وضمنه ملكه بأداء الضمان فتبين أنه أمره ملکه اقتضاء فصح ببيع المرتهن فلا يرجع على الراهن بدينه وإن شاه العدل ملكه، وإنما أداه إلى المرتهن على حساب أن المبيع ملك الراهن فإذا تبين أنه ملكه لم يكن راضياً به فله أن يرجع به عليه. وفي الوجه الأول وهو ما إذا كان قائماً في يد المشتري فللمستحق أن يأخذه من يده لأنه وجد عين ملكه، ثم إن للمشتري أن يرجع على العدل بالثمن لأنه العاقد فتتعلق به حقوق العقد وهذا من حقوقه حيث وجب بالبيع. وإنما دفعه المشتري إليه ليسلم له المبيع ولم يسلم ثم إذا ضمن العدل الثمن للمشتري كان بالخيار إن شاء رجع على الراهن بالقيمة لأنه هو أدخله الذي هذه العهدة فيجب عليه تخليصة، وإذا في رجع عليه صح الرهن وسلم له المقبوض وبرئ الراهن من الدين وإن شاء العدل رجع على المرتهن لأن البيع انتقض بالاستحقاق فبطل الثمن وقد قبضه ثمناً فيجب عليه رده ونقض قبض المرتهن ضرورة، فإذا دفعه إلى العدل عاد حقه في الدين على الراهن كما كان فيرجع به عليه. ولو أن المشتري سلم الثمن بنفسه إلى المرتهن لم يرجع على العدل به لأن العدل في البيع عامل راهن وإنما يرجع عليه إذا قبض ولم يقبض منه شيئاً فبقي ضمان الثمن على المرتهن والدين على الراهن على حاله ولو كان التوكيل بعد عقد الرهن غير مشروط في العقد فالحق يرجع به على الراهن، قبض المرتهن الثمن أو لم يقبض، لأنه لم يتعلق بهذا من العهدة العدل التوكيل حق المرتهن فلا يرجع عليه كما في الوكالة المفردة عن الراهن إذا باع الوكيل ودفع الثمن إلى من أمره الموكل ثم لحقه عهدة لا يرجع على القابض بخلاف الوكالة المشروطة في العقد لأنه تعلق بها حق المرتهن فيكون البيع لحقه ؛ كذا ذكره الكرخي. وهذا يؤيد قول من لا يرى جبر هذا الوكيل على البيع. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : هو ظاهر الرواية لأن رضا المرتهن بالرهن بدون التوكيل قد تم فصار التوكيل مستأنفاً في ضمن عقد الرهن فكان منفصلاً عنه ضرورة على أن فخر الإسلام وشيخ الإسلام قالا قول من يرى جبر هذا الوكيل أصح لإطلاق محمد في الجامع الصغير والأصل ما بيناه فتكون الوكالة غير المشروطة في العقد كالمشروطة فيه في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام هناك. ولم يتعرض المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى لرهن المكاتب والمأذون والمضارب وأحد الشريكين ففي المبسوط : المكاتب كالحر في الرهن والارتهان ورهن العبد التاجر وارتهانه جائز ورهن المضارب على أقسام إما أن يكون رب المال أمره بالاستدانة ولم يأمره بالرهن أو بالعكس أو أمره بهما، فإن أمره بالاستدانة فالرهن جائز . وتفسير الاستدانة أن يشتري بالنسيئة على المضاربة ولم يبق من رأس المال شيء، فإن صار مال
The author, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And if the trustee sold it and gave its price to the mortgagee, then the pawn was claimed and he was liable, the trustee must guarantee the pawnor its value or the mortgagee its price. And the explanation of this is that the sold pawn, if claimed, is either still in existence or destroyed. In the second case (destroyed), the claimant (mustahaqq) has the option: if he wishes, he may claim against the pawnor because he is a usurper in his right by taking or delivering; or if he wishes, he may claim against the trustee because he is a transgressor like him by selling and delivering, thus becoming a usurper thereby. So if the claimant claims against the pawnor, the sale is effective and the recovery (of debt) is valid, because the pawnor owned it by paying the guarantee retroactively to the time of usurpation, so it becomes clear that he ordered him to sell his own property. And if the claimant claimed against the trustee, the sale is also effective because the trustee owned it by paying the guarantee, then he has the option: if he wishes, he may recourse against the pawnor for the value because he is an agent on behalf of him, acting for him in it, so he recourses against him for what befell him of liability due to risk from his side. And the sale is effective because the pawn, since it was the subject of guarantee, and he guaranteed it, he owned it by paying the guarantee, so it becomes clear that he ordered him to sell his property to recover the debt, so the sale by the mortgagee is valid, and he does not recourse against the pawnor for his debt. And if he wishes, the trustee recourses against the mortgagee for the price, because it becomes clear that he took the price without right, because the trustee owned the slave by paying the guarantee, and his ownership was established in it, and it did not transfer to the pawnor on the assumption that he does not recourse against the pawnor for what he guaranteed, because he was the direct agent. So the price becomes his, because it is the compensation for his property. He paid it to the mortgagee believing that the sold item was the pawnor's property; when it becomes clear that it is his property, he is not satisfied with it, so he may recourse against him for it. In the first case, which is when it is still in the hand of the buyer, the claimant may take it from his hand because he found his own property in kind. Then the buyer may recourse against the trustee for the price, because he is the contracting party, so the rights of the contract attach to him, and this is among his rights, as it became obligatory by the sale. He paid it to him so that the sold item would be delivered to him, but it was not delivered. Then if the trustee guarantees the price to the buyer, he has the option: if he wishes, he recourses against the pawnor for the value, because it is he who brought him into this liability, so he must free him from it. And if he recourses against him, the pawn becomes valid, the received item is delivered to him, and the pawnor is released from the debt. And if the trustee wishes, he recourses against the mortgagee because the sale was rescinded by the claim, so the price becomes void, and he had received it as a price, so he must return it, and the taking of the mortgagee is necessarily undone. So if he pays it to the trustee, his right in the debt returns against the pawnor as before, so he recourses against him. And if the buyer himself delivered the price to the mortgagee, he does not recourse against the trustee for it, because the trustee in the sale is an agent of the pawnor, and he only recourses against him if he collected (the price) and he did not collect anything from him, so the guarantee of the price remains on the mortgagee and the debt on the pawnor as it was. And if the authorization was after the pledge contract, not stipulated in the contract, then the right recourses against the pawnor, whether the mortgagee received the price or not, because no right of the mortgagee is attached to this authorization, so he does not recourse against him, as in an independent agency from the pawnor: if the agent sells and pays the price to whomever the principal ordered, then a claim befalls, he does not recourse against the recipient, unlike the authorization stipulated in the contract because the right of the mortgagee is attached to it, so the sale is for his right. Thus mentioned al-Karkhi. And this supports the opinion of those who do not see compulsion of this agent to sell. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: It is apparent riwayah because the consent of the mortgagee to the pawn without authorization was completed, so the authorization is initiated within the pledge contract, thus it is separate from it by necessity. On the other hand, Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam said the opinion of those who see compulsion of this agent is more correct, due to the generalization of Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir and the principle we explained, so the authorization not stipulated in the contract is like that stipulated in it in all the rulings we mentioned there. And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, did not address the pawn of the mukatab, the ma'dhun, the mudarib, and one of two partners. In al-Mabsut: the mukatab is like a free person in giving and taking pawns; the pawn of a trading slave and his taking a pawn are valid; the pawn of a mudarib is of types: either the owner of capital ordered him to borrow and did not order him to pawn, or vice versa, or ordered him to both. If he ordered him to borrow, then the pawn is valid. And the interpretation of borrowing (istidāna) is to buy on credit for the mudaraba while nothing remains of the capital; if there is money...
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the ruling presented earlier, now attributed to the author of the matn (Nasafi). It breaks down the scenario of the sold pawn being claimed into two cases: the pawn is either destroyed (hālik) or still in existence (qā'im). For the first case (destroyed), the claimant (mustahaqq) has a choice: he can claim compensation from the pawnor (rahin) because the pawnor is considered a usurper (ghāṣib) in relation to the claimant's right, whether by taking or delivering the pawn. Alternatively, the claimant can claim from the trustee ('adl), who also became a usurper by selling and delivering. If the claimant chooses to claim from the pawnor, the sale of the pawn remains valid and the mortgagee's recovery of his debt is valid, because the pawnor's payment of compensation retroactively establishes that he owned the pawn at the time of the sale—so the trustee was actually selling the pawnor's own property. If the claimant claims from the trustee, the trustee pays compensation and then becomes the owner of the pawn. The trustee then has a choice: he can seek recourse against the pawnor for the value of the pawn, claiming as an agent who suffered loss due to the pawnor's defect (ghurar), or he can seek recourse against the mortgagee for the price he received, because it becomes clear that the mortgagee took the price without right. The second case (pawn still exists) involves the buyer returning the pawn to the claimant. The buyer then sues the trustee for the price. The trustee, after paying, again has a choice: recourse against the pawnor (to recover the value) or against the mortgagee (to recover the price). The passage concludes by discussing the effect of authorization (tawkīl) on recourse rights, preferring the view that the trustee is not compelled to sell if authorization was not stipulated, but also noting the stronger view that compulsion applies absolutely. Finally, it notes that the author omitted rulings on certain categories of pawnors (mukatab, ma'dhun, mudarib, and partners), referring to al-Mabsut for details.

Key Terms
مستحق (mustahaqq): Claimant; a third party who proves ownership of the pawned item.
غاصب (ghāṣib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes or causes loss of another's property.
عهدة ('uhda): Liability or warranty arising from a sale or contract.
عدل ('adl): Trustee; the neutral party who holds the pawn and may sell it upon maturity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pawn is destroyed and the claimant chooses to claim compensation from the trustee, what recourse does the trustee have against the mortgagee?
A) None, because the mortgagee is not liable
B) He can recover the price from the mortgagee because it was taken without right
C) He can only recover from the pawnor
D) He can only recover from the buyer

Key Takeaway
The trustee's liability and recourse rights hinge on whether the pawn was destroyed or exists, and on the nature of his authorization; the mufta bihi view gives him full recourse against the mortgagee.

Recourses When Pledged Property Sold by the Trustee Is Claimed by a Third Party
Recourses after istihqaq of pledged property sold by trustee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the chain of recourses (buyer, trustee, mortgagee, pledgor) when pledged property sold by a trustee (عدل) is reclaimed by a third party, and distinguish the effect of direct payment to the mortgagee versus payment to the trustee.

Core Idea
The trustee sells his own property after paying the debt, so the sale is valid; recourses depend on who received the price.

When a trustee (عدل) sells pledged property that later turns out to belong to a third party, several legal recourses arise. The sale is valid because the trustee, by paying the debt, becomes owner and sells his own property. This lesson traces the rights of the buyer, trustee, mortgagee, and pledgor in three scenarios: the property still with the buyer, the buyer paying the trustee, and the buyer paying the mortgagee directly. We also encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the trustee can be compelled to sell when agency was not stipulated in the pledge contract.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of sale validity
Identify why the trustee's sale is valid: the trustee pays the debt and becomes owner of the pledged item. Thus he sells his own property. The sale is executed (نفذ البيع) and the trustee does not recover the debt from the pledgor.
This is the key principle that underlies all recourses.
2
Trace the first scenario: property still in buyer's hand
The rightful owner takes the property from the buyer. The buyer then sues the trustee for the price because the trustee was the contractor and the buyer paid the price to him expecting delivery.
3
Analyze the trustee's choice after compensating the buyer
After the trustee pays the buyer, he may sue the pledgor for the value (since the pledgor caused the liability) or sue the mortgagee (since the sale is voided and the mortgagee must return the price).
The trustee cannot recover from both; he chooses one.
4
Examine the effect of direct payment to the mortgagee
If the buyer paid the mortgagee directly (not through the trustee), the buyer does not sue the trustee. The mortgagee is liable to return the price, and the pledgor remains indebted.
This is because the trustee did not receive the price.
5
Distinguish cases of stipulated vs. non-stipulated agency
If the agency to sell was stipulated in the pledge contract, the mortgagee's right attaches, and the buyer can sue the mortgagee. If not stipulated, al-Karkhi and al-Sarakhsi hold that the agency is separate, so the mortgagee is not liable.
This leads to an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the trustee can be compelled to sell.
6
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Two views: (1) The trustee cannot be compelled (al-Karkhi, al-Sarakhsi) because consent to the pledge without agency is complete. (2) The trustee can be compelled (Fakhr al-Islam, Shaykh al-Islam) based on absolute statements of Imam Muhammad. The author favors the latter as 'asahh' (sounder).
This difference affects whether the trustee must sell even if not initially authorized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن باب الرهن يوضع على يد عدل جهته عامل له فيه فيرجع عليه بما لحقه من العهدة بالغرر من جهته ونفذ البيع لأن الرهن لما كان مدار الضمان عليه وضمنه ملكه بأداء الضمان فتبين أنه أمره ملکه اقتضاء فصح ببيع المرتهن فلا يرجع على الراهن بدينه وإن شاء العدل رجع على المرتهن بالثمن لأنه تبين أن الثمن أخذه بغير حق لأن العدل ملك العبد بأداء الضمان واستقر ملكه فيه ولم ينتقل إلى الراهن على تقدير أن لا يرجع على الراهن بما ضمن لأنه المباشر فصار الثمن له لأنه بدل ملكه، وإنما أداه إلى المرتهن على حساب أن المبيع ملك الراهن فإذا تبين أنه ملكه لم يكن راضياً به فله أن يرجع به عليه. وفي الوجه الأول وهو ما إذا كان قائماً في يد المشتري فللمستحق أن يأخذه من يده لأنه وجد عين ملكه، ثم إن للمشتري أن يرجع على العدل بالثمن لأنه العاقد فتتعلق به حقوق العقد وهذا من حقوقه حيث وجب بالبيع. وإنما دفعه المشتري إليه ليسلم له المبيع ولم يسلم ثم إذا ضمن العدل الثمن للمشتري كان بالخيار إن شاء رجع على الراهن بالقيمة لأنه هو أدخله في هذه العهدة فيجب عليه تخليصه، وإذا رجع عليه صح الرهن وسلم له المقبوض وبرئ الراهن من الدين وإن شاء العدل رجع على المرتهن لأن البيع انتقض بالاستحقاق فبطل الثمن وقد قبضه ثمناً فيجب عليه رده ونقض قبض المرتهن ضرورة، فإذا دفعه إلى العدل عاد حقه في الدين على الراهن كما كان فيرجع به عليه. ولو أن المشتري سلم الثمن بنفسه إلى المرتهن لم يرجع على العدل به لأن العدل في البيع عامل راهن وإنما يرجع عليه إذا قبض ولم يقبض منه شيئاً فبقي ضمان الثمن على المرتهن والدين على الراهن على حاله ولو كان التوكيل بعد عقد الرهن غير مشروط في العقد فالحق يرجع به على الراهن، قبض المرتهن الثمن أو لم يقبض، لأنه لم يتعلق بهذا من العهدة التوكيل حق المرتهن فلا يرجع عليه كما في الوكالة المفردة عن الراهن إذا باع الوكيل ودفع الثمن إلى من أمره الموكل ثم لحقه عهدة لا يرجع على القابض بخلاف الوكالة المشروطة في العقد لأنه تعلق بها حق المرتهن فيكون البيع لحقه ؛ كذا ذكره الكرخي. وهذا يؤيد قول من لا يرى جبر هذا الوكيل على البيع. وقال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : هو ظاهر الرواية لأن رضا المرتهن بالرهن بدون التوكيل قد تم فصار التوكيل مستأنفاً في ضمن عقد الرهن فكان منفصلاً عنه ضرورة على أن فخر الإسلام وشيخ الإسلام قالا قول من يرى جبر هذا الوكيل أصح لإطلاق محمد في الجامع الصغير والأصل ما بيناه فتكون الوكالة غير المشروطة في العقد كالمشروطة فيه في جميع ما ذكرنا من الأحكام هناك.
Book of Pledge, Chapter on Placing a Pledge with a Trustee. [If] his agent [=the trustee] acts in it [=sells], then he [the trustee] is sued for the liability that befell him due to the defect from his side, and the sale is executed because since the pledge is based on guarantee, and he guaranteed it, he owned it by paying the guarantee, so it becomes clear that he [the trustee] ordered [the sale of] his property by way of recovery, so the sale by the mortgagee is valid, and he [the trustee] does not sue the pledgor for his debt. And if the trustee wishes, he sues the mortgagee for the price, because it is clear that he took the price without right, because the trustee owned the slave by paying the guarantee and his ownership was established in it, and it did not transfer to the pledgor on the assumption that he [the trustee] does not sue the pledgor for what he guaranteed because he [the trustee] is the direct actor, so the price belongs to him because it is the substitute for his property; he only paid it to the mortgagee on the basis that the sold item belonged to the pledgor, and when it becomes clear that it is his property, he [the trustee] was not consenting, so he has the right to sue him [the mortgagee] for it. In the first case, which is when it [the property] is still in the buyer's hand, the rightful claimant takes it from his hand because he found his own property in kind. Then the buyer has the right to sue the trustee for the price, because he is the contractor, so the rights of the contract attach to him, and this is among his rights since the price was obligatory by the sale. The buyer paid it to him [the trustee] only to have the sold item delivered to him, and it was not delivered. Then, if the trustee pays the price to the buyer, he has a choice: if he wishes, he sues the pledgor for the value, because he [the trustee] is the one who brought him into this liability, so he must free him; and if he sues him [the pledgor], the pledge is valid, the received property is delivered to him, and the pledgor is freed from the debt. If the trustee wishes, he sues the mortgagee, because the sale was invalidated by the claim, so the price became void, and he had received it as a price, so he must return it, and the mortgagee's possession is necessarily annulled. When he pays it to the trustee, his right to the debt against the pledgor returns as it was, so he sues him for it. If the buyer paid the price himself to the mortgagee, he does not sue the trustee for it, because the trustee in the sale is the pledgor's agent; he only sues him if he [the trustee] received payment, and he did not receive anything from him [the buyer], so the guarantee of the price remains on the mortgagee and the debt on the pledgor as it was. If the agency [to sell] after the pledge contract was not stipulated in the contract, then the right is sued against the pledgor, whether the mortgagee received the price or not, because the agency did not attach the mortgagee's right to this liability, so he [the mortgagee] is not sued, as in a separate agency from the pledgor: if the agent sells and pays the price to the one the principal commanded, then a claim befalls him, he does not sue the recipient, unlike the agency stipulated in the contract, because the mortgagee's right attached to it, so the sale is for his right; thus mentioned by al-Karkhi. And this supports the view of those who do not see compelling this agent to sell. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: It is the apparent narration because the mortgagee's consent to the pledge without the agency has been completed, so the agency becomes independent within the pledge contract, so it is necessarily separate from it. However, Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam said that the view of those who see compelling this agent is sounder due to the absolute statement of Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Asl as we explained, so the agency not stipulated in the contract is like the stipulated one in all the rulings we mentioned there.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the scenario where a trustee (عدل) appointed to sell pledged property sells it, but the property is later found to belong to a third party (istihqaq). The author explains the various recourses available. The sale is valid because the trustee, by paying off the debt, becomes owner of the pledged item and sells his own property. Therefore, the trustee cannot recover the debt from the pledgor. However, the trustee may recover the price from the mortgagee because the mortgagee took it without right, as the trustee owned the item. If the property is still in the buyer's hand, the rightful owner takes it directly from the buyer. The buyer then sues the trustee for the price. The trustee, having paid the buyer, has a choice: he may sue the pledgor for the value (since the pledgor caused the liability) or sue the mortgagee (since the sale is voided). If the buyer paid the mortgagee directly, he has no recourse against the trustee. The passage then contrasts cases where the agency was stipulated in the pledge contract versus cases where it was not. Al-Karkhi holds that if the agency was not stipulated, the trustee cannot be compelled to sell, and the mortgagee cannot sue the trustee for the price. Al-Sarakhsi supports this as the apparent narration. However, Fakhr al-Islam and Shaykh al-Islam side with the view that the agent can be compelled, citing Imam Muhammad's general statements. Thus, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the effect of the agency clause.

Key Terms
العدل (al-'adl): A trusted third party (trustee) who holds the pledged property and may be authorized to sell it upon default.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): A third party's rightful claim to property that was sold, proving the seller lacked ownership.
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in the transaction, here referring to the liability the trustee incurs from selling without clear title.
الوكالة (al-wakala): Agency; the authorization given to the trustee to sell the pledged property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the trustee (عدل) sells pledged property and the buyer pays the price directly to the mortgagee, whom can the buyer sue if the property is reclaimed?
A) The trustee only
B) The mortgagee only
C) The pledgor only
D) Either the trustee or the mortgagee

Key Takeaway
The trustee's sale is valid because he becomes owner by paying the debt; recourses for the buyer, trustee, mortgagee, and pledgor depend on who received the price and whether the agency was stipulated.
