Partial Redemption, Multiple Lenders, and Death of Parties in Pawn of Borrowed Items
Rulings on partial repayment, post-redemption liability, and death of parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why partial repayment applies to the entire pawn in Hanafi law and evaluate the preferred narration regarding liability after redemption.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a pawn cannot be held in shares; thus even partial repayment applies to the whole item, and after full redemption the pledgee is liable for its loss.

This lesson covers two important scenarios: when a borrower pawns an item from two lenders and repays only half, and when the pawn is fully redeemed but perishes before return. The Hanafi school addresses these with its characteristic precision to avoid shuyu' (undivided shares) and to ensure justice among parties. We will also examine rulings on death of parties and the procedure when the lender has debts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prohibition of shuyu' in pawn
The text states that making each part of a pawn held for part of the debt would cause shuyu' (undivided shares) which invalidates the pawn. Therefore, each part is held for the entire debt.
Shuyu' is prohibited in rahn because the pawn must be specifically identifiable.
2
Analyze the partial repayment scenario
If a man borrows from two people to pawn, then repays half the debt, the repayment applies to the entire pawn, not to a specific half. This avoids shuyu'.
The borrower cannot say 'this half is for lender A' because that would create shares.
3
Trace the dispute on liability after full redemption
When the pawn is redeemed (debt paid) and then perishes with the pledgee, the pledgee pays its value. Two narrations: one says pay the owner (mu'ir), another (Abu Hafs) says pay the pledgor who then pays the lender. The text declares the latter correct.
The reasoning: the lender is deemed to have received repayment from the time of pawn, so the pledgee should return to the pledgor.
4
Understand the ruling on death of lender and borrower
If the lender (mu'ir) and borrower (musta'ir) die, the heirs cannot unilaterally recover the pawn because that would remove the pledgee's right of possession.
The pawn remains with the pledgee until other matters are resolved.
5
Apply the procedure when the lender has debts and the pawn has surplus
If the lender owes debts and the pawn is worth more than the borrower's debt, it is not sold immediately. Instead, creditors and heirs gather because there is hope the borrower may repay his own debt or be released, preserving the surplus for the heirs.
The judge's role is to protect rights, not to hastily liquidate.

Source Text

A) Directly to the lender (mu'ir)

Guarantee for Injury upon the Pledge by Either Party
Author states and proves liability for damage to a pledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that injury to the pledge by either party is guaranteed, explain the proof via qiyas to a slave bequeathed for service, and apply the conditions for the mortgagee to take compensation.

Core Idea
Damage to a pledge by either pledgor or mortgagee must be compensated because each party's right is respected.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal consequence when the pledgor (owner) or mortgagee (creditor) damages the pledged property. The author not only states the rule but also provides a qiyas (analogical proof) from a slave bequeathed for service, showing how the Hanafi school derives this liability from the respect of contractual rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and parties
The text states 'وجناية الراهن والمرتهن على الرهن مضمونة' – injury by either party to the pledge is guaranteed (liable for compensation). Narrow down the two parties: the pledgor (owner) and the mortgagee (creditor-holder).
The term 'مضمونة' means the perpetrator must pay compensation (daman).
2
Understand the basis: respect of rights
The reason given is that each party has a respected right (حق محترم) in the pledge. The pledgor has ownership, and the mortgagee has a binding right of possession. Because these rights are respected, damaging the pledge that harms the other's right obligates compensation.
The mortgagee's right is described as 'حق لازم' (binding right), like a partial ownership interest.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogical proof)
The text analogizes to a slave whose services are bequeathed. If the heirs destroy that slave, they must guarantee his value to buy a replacement, because the legatee's right to the usufruct is respected. Similarly, the mortgagee's right to hold the pledge is respected, so the owner is treated like a stranger regarding guarantee.
The qiyas is jaliy (clear) because the illa (effective cause) is the respect of a third party's right in the property.
4
Determine how the mortgagee takes compensation
The text specifies: if the compensation is of the same type as the debt and the debt is due, the mortgagee takes it as payment. If the debt is deferred, he detains the compensation until the debt matures. If the type differs, he detains it until the debt is paid.
This shows that the compensation is not automatically owned by the mortgagee; it is tied to the debt's conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وجناية الراهن والمرتهن على الرهن مضمونة) لأن حق كل واحد منهما يحترم فيجب عليه ضمان ما أتلف على صاحبه لأن الراهن مالك وقد تعدى عليه المرتهن فيضمنه والمرتهن حقه لازم محترم وتعلق مثله بالمال فيجعل المالك كأجنبي في حق الضمان كالعبد الموصى بخدمته إذا أتلفه الورثة ضمنوا قيمته ليشتري به عبداً يقوم مقام الأول، وهذا يمنع المريض من التبرع بأكثر من ثم المرتهن يأخذ الضمان بدينه إن كان من جنس دينه وكان الدين حالاً، وإن كان مؤجلاً يحبسه بالدين فإذا حل بدينه إن كان من جنس حقه وإلا حبسه بدينه حتى يستوفي دينه.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the injury of the pledgor and the mortgagee upon the pledge is guaranteed) because the right of each of them is respected, so it is obligatory upon him to guarantee what he destroyed of his companion's property. Because the pledgor is an owner, and the mortgagee has encroached upon him, so he makes him liable. And the mortgagee has a binding and respected right, and its like attaches to property, so the owner is treated like a non-owner regarding guarantee, like a slave bequeathed for his service; if the heirs destroy him, they guarantee his value to buy a slave who takes the place of the first. And this prevents the patient from donating more than [the bequest]. Then the mortgagee takes the guarantee for his debt if it is of the same type as his debt and the debt is due; if it is deferred, he detains it against the debt. When it becomes due, [he takes it] for his debt if it is of the same type as his right; otherwise he detains it against his debt until he receives his debt.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) providing the ruling and its proof. The ruling: Injury to the pledge by either the pledgor (owner) or mortgagee (creditor-holder) is guaranteed (madhmun), meaning the injured party must compensate the other for damage to the pledge. The proof: Each party has a respected right in the pledge—the pledgor his ownership, the mortgagee his right of possession (habs). Since these rights are respected (muhtaram), any damage caused to the pledge that harms the other's right must be compensated. The analogy is made to a slave whose usufruct (services) is bequeathed; if the heirs destroy the slave, they must pay his value to buy a replacement, because the right of the legatee is respected. Similarly, the mortgagee's right is respected, so the pledgor (owner) is treated like a stranger concerning guarantee. The text then explains how the mortgagee takes the guarantee: if the compensation is of the same type as the debt and the debt is due, he takes it as payment; if the debt is deferred, he detains the compensation until the debt matures. If the type differs, he detains it until the debt is paid.

Key Terms
مضمونة (madhmunah): Guaranteed or liable for compensation; injury to the pledge must be compensated by the perpetrator.
حق لازم (haqq lazim): A binding right; the mortgagee's right to hold the pledge is binding and cannot be unilaterally revoked.
كأجنبي (ka-ajnabi): Like a stranger; the owner is treated as a non-owner regarding liability because the mortgagee's right attaches to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pledgor (owner) destroys the pledge, to whom is the compensation paid?
A) To the mortgagee directly as a gift
B) To the mortgagee as payment or detention against his debt
C) To the pledgor himself
D) To the judge for charity

Key Takeaway
In a pledge (rahn), damage caused by either party to the pledged property is compensable because the rights of both are respected, with the mortgagee's right overriding the owner's absolute control.

Pledged Slave Kills a Free Man: Ransom and Liability When Value Equals or Is Less Than Debt
Ruling on a pledged slave killing a free man by mistake when value equals or is less than debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a pledged slave commits qatl khata' and the slave's value equals or is less than the debt, and explain why the mortgagee bears the ransom.

Core Idea
When a pledged slave kills, the mortgagee bears the ransom because destruction is upon him.

This lesson examines the first scenario from a detailed passage in al-Mabsut: a pledged slave kills a free man by mistake. The ruling depends on whether the slave's value equals, is less than, or exceeds the debt. Here we focus on the case where the value equals or is less than the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal consequence
The act is qatl khata' (wrongful killing) by a pledged slave. Determine the options: either the slave is handed over to the victim's family (daf') or a ransom (fida') is paid to free the slave.
The victim's family has the right to choose one.
2
Note the three value scenarios relative to debt
The value of the slave may equal the debt, be less than the debt, or exceed the debt. Each scenario has different rules. Here, value equals or is less than debt.
If value is less, the debt might be partly secured.
3
For value = debt: both parties addressed with daf' or fida'
Both the pledgor and mortgagee must agree because they have competing rights. The mortgagee has a right resembling ownership (right of detention), so their consensus is required.
If both choose daf', the debt is voided as if the slave died.
4
If one chooses daf' and the other refuses
No daf' is possible because the willing party cannot force the other. If the mortgagee chooses ransom, the entire ransom is upon him, by analogy to a usurped slave and medicine.
The mortgagee's rationale: he preserves his right by ransoming.
5
If the pledgor ransoms
The pledgor's payment counts as full or partial debt repayment. He can recover the ransom from the mortgagee if necessary because he is not a volunteer; he is improving his ownership.
If pledged slave dies after ransom, ransom is returned to pledgor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لا يحتمل النقص والإسناد لا يظهر في حق التصرفات التي لا تحتمل النقص فاحتمل فاقتصر الاستيفاء بالهلاك على الحال، وإن كان الاستيفاء بالهلاك آخرهما فيرد ما استوفاه آخراً وصار كما لو رهن بالمهر أو ببدل And the author did not address when the pledge injures a free stranger. He said in al-Mabsut: A slave who is a pledge killed a man by mistake. This is either such that its value equals the debt, or less, or more. If its value equals the debt, then the pledgor and the mortgagee are both addressed with regard to handing over (daf') or ransom (fida'), because one has the reality of ownership and the other has a right that resembles true ownership, so they both stand as opponents, and their consensus is required in the address of handing over or ransom. If they both hand over, the debt is voided because the slave leaves the pledgor's ownership due to a cause while in the mortgagee's hand and in his guarantee, so it becomes as if it died naturally, so the (debt) collection is confirmed. If one chooses handing over and the other refuses, it is not handed over. Because if the pledgor chooses handing over, he seeks to remove the pledgor's ownership without his consent, so he is prevented. And if the mortgagee chooses ransom, then the entire ransom is upon the mortgagee because the ransom is to ward off destruction from the slave and he revives his right by purifying it from the injury, like taking medicine to ward off destruction and the cost of the medicine is upon him because destruction is upon him; similarly the ransom. And it becomes like a usurped slave who injures: the injury is against the usurper because destruction is upon him; similarly this. And he does not recover the ransom from the pledgor because he has fulfilled an obligation upon him. And if the pledgor ransoms him, it is a payment of the debt if the ransom covers the entire debt, and it does not remain a pledge; if it covers part, then to that extent because he is not a volunteer in the ransom because it involves improving his ownership and extracting his right. And the slave is occupied with the injury, and the slave appears from the injury and revives his ownership, and the owner is not described as a volunteer in improving his ownership and reviving it. So he has fulfilled an obligation upon the mortgagee and he is compelled in it, so he has recourse against him. Like one who lends him [something] at the time of his pledging for his debt, then the lender pays the borrower's debt, he recovers from the borrower because he needs to free his ownership and purify it from the encumbrance of the pledge; similarly this. And if it [the slave] is destroyed in the mortgagee's hand after the pledgor ransomed him, the ransom is returned to the pledgor because the pledge becomes free of the debt by payment, because he has become a payer of his debt by the ransom. Some of our shaikhs said: he returns the thousand [that was] collected upon the destruction of the pledge, and what was found after the thousand is not attributed to the time of the pledge because the ransom has the effect of injury, and injury is a real action that does not admit of deficiency, and attribution does not appear in the case of actions that do not admit deficiency, so it becomes possible, so the collection by destruction is limited to the present. And if the collection by destruction is the latter of the two, then he returns what he collected last. And it becomes as if he pledged for the mahr or for the substitute of khul', then the mortgagee collected his debt, then the pledge is destroyed in his hand; he returns what he received because the payment of mahr is subject to revocation. And if the cause of the debt's obligation does not admit revocation, like marriage and khul', similarly this. All this is when ransom or handing over is chosen. If one chooses ransom and the other chooses handing over, then ransom is preferable because the one who chose handing over is capricious. As for the pledgor, because in handing over there is nullification of the mortgagee's right of detention, and his ownership of the slave does not cease, and his ownership of the ransom ceases to a substitute, for he recovers it from the mortgagee. So ransom is more beneficial to him than handing over. And the mortgagee by handing over intends to harm the pledgor without any benefit to himself, because his debt is remitted in both cases, and in handing over there is removal of the pledgor's ownership, while in ransom there is preservation of his ownership. So he is capricious, and no consideration is given to the choice of one who is capricious. All this is when the value of the pledge equals the debt or is less. If it is more, e.g., the slave's value is two thousand and the debt is one thousand, then if they choose ransom, the ransom is upon both halves because one half is guaranteed upon the mortgagee and the other half is a trust in his hand. So the guarantee is apportioned to the mortgagee and the trust to the pledgor, by considering the part as the whole. If the pledgor ransoms, he is a volunteer if the pledgor is present. If he is absent, he recovers half from him according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not recover in either case because he has paid a debt for another without his permission and he is not compelled in it, because the pledgor is forced to ransom the half when the mortgagee permits the ransom, and he does not improve his ownership nor revive his right because he has no ownership in the slave and no right in the half trust; unlike the pledgor. And he has [the argument] that the mortgagee has the right of detention and holding in the half trust if it is not guaranteed upon him, and he needs to revive his right and improve it, and in the ransom there is a revival of his right from one aspect, for he may reach his right by holding it, so he needs the ransom, so he is not described as a volunteer, so he needs the ransom. And if the pledgor ransoms while the mortgagee is absent, he is not a volunteer by agreement, and it leaves the pledge because he improves his own ownership and revives his right, and the owner in improving his ownership is not a volunteer, unless the mortgagee wishes to pay half the ransom. And if the pledgor hands him over, then the mortgagee has [the right to] ...
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on a detailed case not covered by the original author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It is taken from al-Mabsut by Imam al-Sarakhsi. The case: a pledged slave kills a free man by mistake (qatl khata'). The ruling examines the options of handing over the slave to the victim's family (daf') or paying ransom (fida') to free him. The analysis is divided based on the slave's value relative to the debt. (1) If value equals debt: Both pledgor and mortgagee must agree on handing over or ransom. If both hand over, the debt is cancelled. If one refuses, the other cannot force it. If ransom is chosen, it is entirely on the mortgagee because he benefits by preserving his right. If the pledgor ransoms, it counts as payment of the debt to the extent of the ransom, and he can recover from the mortgagee if necessary. If the slave dies after pledgor's ransom, the ransom is returned because the debt is considered paid. (2) If value exceeds debt: The ransom is split equally because half the value is under the mortgagee's guarantee (equivalent to debt) and half is a trust (amanah) with him. If the pledgor ransoms, the issue of volunteerism (tabarru') arises. Abu Hanifa says if the pledgor is absent, he can recover half from the mortgagee; if present, he is a volunteer. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say he cannot recover in either case because he is not compelled. The text gives the reasoning for each position. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing further scenarios.

Key Terms
الفداء (al-fida'): Ransom paid to free a slave from liability for homicide; here, it refers to paying the victim's family to avoid handing over the slave.
الدفع (al-daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's family as compensation for the killing; results in loss of the slave and cancellation of debt.
متعنت (muta'annit): Capricious or obstinate; one who chooses an option that harms the other party without benefit to himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a pledged slave's value equals the debt, if the mortgagee chooses daf' (handover) and the pledgor chooses fida' (ransom), what is the outcome?
A) The mortgagee's choice prevails because he has a right of detention.
B) The pledgor's choice prevails because the mortgagee is capricious.
C) The case goes to the judge to decide.
D) The slave is freed without any payment.

Key Takeaway
When a pledged slave kills by mistake and the value equals or is less than the debt, the mortgagee bears the full ransom if he chooses it, and the pledgor's ransom counts as debt payment with recourse.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Pledgor's Right to Recover Ransom When Value Exceeds Debt
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the pledgor's recourse when ransoming a pledge with value exceeding debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the pledgor's recovery of half the ransom when the slave's value exceeds the debt, and apply the principle that the choice of a capricious person is invalid.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the mortgagee's presence; the Sahibayn consider the pledgor a volunteer.

When the pledged slave's value exceeds the debt, the ransom is split in half because half the value is under guarantee and half is a trust. A dispute arises: if the pledgor pays the entire ransom, can he recover half from the mortgagee? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes if the mortgagee is absent; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario: value exceeds debt
E.g., slave worth 2000, debt 1000. Half the value (1000) is guaranteed (under mortgagee's liability), half (1000) is a trust (amanah) with the mortgagee.
This division is crucial for liability.
2
If both choose ransom: split half
If both parties agree to ransom, the ransom (the slave's full value) is paid half by each, because the mortgagee's guarantee covers half.
This is the starting point.
3
If the pledgor ransoms alone: two views
Abu Hanifa: if mortgagee absent, pledgor recovers half; if present, he is a volunteer. Sahibayn: no recovery in either case.
The core dispute.
4
Abu Hanifa's reasoning
The mortgagee has a right of detention in the half trust. When absent, the pledgor is compelled to pay to revive his own ownership. When present, the pledgor could have forced the mortgagee to pay half, so paying without compulsion is voluntary.
Presence/absence changes compulsion.
5
Sahibayn's reasoning
The pledgor is paying a debt (the mortgagee's share) without permission and is not compelled because the mortgagee can be forced to pay half if the pledgor chooses ransom. Thus the pledgor is a volunteer.
They focus on the pledgor's ability to compel.
6
Apply the muta'annit principle
Earlier in the text, if one chooses handover and the other ransom, the ransom is preferred because the handover chooser is capricious. This principle also applies here: the mortgagee who refuses to ransom when value exceeds debt may be muta'annit.
But the dispute here is about recovery after pledgor pays alone.

Source Text

فالفداء كله على المرتهن لأن الفداء لدفع الهلاك عن العبد وأحيا به حقه لتطهيره عن الجناية كاتخاذ الدواء لدفع الهلاك وثمن الدواء عليه لأن الهلاك عليه فكذلك الفداء وصار كالعبد المغصوب إذا جنى فالجناية على الغصب لأن الهلاك عليه فكذا هذا، ولا يرجع بالفداء على الراهن لأنه قضى حقاً واجباً عليه. وإن فداه الراهن كان قضاء بالدين إن بلغ الفداء كل الدين ولا يبقى رهناً، وإن بلغ بعضه فبقدره لأنه غير متبرع في الفداء لأن فيه استصلاح ملكه واستخلاص حقه فإن العبد مشغول بالجناية والعبد يظهر عن الجناية ويحيي ملكه والمالك لا يوصف بالتبرع في اصلاح ملكه وإحيائه فقد قضى واجباً على المرتهن وهو مضطر فيه فكان له الرجوع عليه كمن إعاره عند رهنه بدينه ثم قضى المعير دين المستعير يرجع بما قضى على المستعير لأنه يحتاج إلى تخليص ملكه فيطهره عن شغل الرهن فكذا ،هذا فإن هلك في يد المرتهن بعدما فداه الراهن يرد على الراهن الفداء لأن الرهن بريء عن الدين بالإيفاء لأنه صار موفياً دينه بالفداء . قال بعض مشايخنا إنه يرد الألف المستوفاة بهلاك الرهن وما وجد بعد الألف لم يستند إلى وقت الرهن لأن للفداء حكم الجناية والجناية فعل حقيقي لا يحتمل النقص والإسناد لا يظهر في حق التصرفات التي لا تحتمل النقص فاحتمل فاقتصر الاستيفاء بالهلاك على الحال، وإن كان الاستيفاء بالهلاك آخرهما فيرد ما استوفاه آخراً وصار كما لو رهن بالمهر أو ببدل الخلع ثم استوفى المرتهن دينه ثم هلك الرهن في يده يرد ما قبض لأن قضاء المهر يحتمل النقض. وإن كان سبب وجوب الدين لا يحتمل النقض وهو النكاح والخلع فكذا هذا كله إذا اختار الفداء أو الدفع، فإن اختار أحدهما الفداء والآخر الدفع فالفداء أولى لأن الذي اختار الدفع متعنت فيه ؛ أما الراهن فلأن في الدفع إبطال حق المرتهن في الحبس ولا يزول ملكه عن العبد ويزول ملكه عن الفداء إلى خلف فإنه يرجع به على المرتهن فكان الفداء له أنفع من الدفع والمرتهن بالدفع قصد إلحاق الضرر بالراهن من غير نفع يحصل له لأن دينه يسقط في الحالين وفي الدفع إزالة ملك الراهن، وفي الفداء إبقاؤه على ملكه فكان متعنتاً ولا عبرة لاختيار المتعنت هذا كله إذا كانت قيمة الرهن مثل الدين أو أقل، فإن كانت أكثر بأن كانت قيمة العبد ألفين والدين ألف، فإن اختار الفداء فالفداء عليهما نصفين لأن نصفه مضمون على المرتهن ونصفه أمانة عنده فيقدر الضمان على المرتهن وتقدر الأمانة على الراهن اعتباراً للبعض بالكل فإن فداه الراهن فهو متبرع إن كان الراهن حاضراً، وإن كان غائباً يرجع على الراهن بنصفه عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى، وعندهما لا يرجع في الحالين لأنه قضى ديناً عن غيره بغير أمه وهو غير مضطر فيه لأن الراهن يجبر على فداء النصف متى أجاز المرتهن الفداء ولا يصلح ملكه ولا يحيي حقه لأنه لا ملك له في العبد ولا حق له في نصف الأمانة ولا كذلك الراهن. وله أن للمرتهن في نصف الأمانة حق الحبس والإمساك إن لم يكن مضموناً عليه وهو محتاج إلى إحياء حقه وإصلاحه وفي الفداء إحياء حقه من وجه فإن يصل إلى حقه بإمساكه فيكون محتاجاً إلى الفداء فلا يوصف بالتبرع فيحتاج إلى الفداء، ولو فداه الراهن والمرتهن غائب لم يكن متطوعاً اتفاقاً وخرج عن الرهن لأنه يصلح ملك نفسه ويحيي حقه والمالك في إصلاح ملكه لا يكون متبرعاً إلا أن يشاء المرتهن أن يؤدي نصف الفداء ولو دفعه الراهن فللمرتهن إن
And the author did not address when the pledge injures a free stranger. He said in al-Mabsut: A slave who is a pledge killed a man by mistake. This is either such that its value equals the debt, or less, or more. If its value equals the debt, then the pledgor and the mortgagee are both addressed with regard to handing over (daf') or ransom (fida'), because one has the reality of ownership and the other has a right that resembles true ownership, so they both stand as opponents, and their consensus is required in the address of handing over or ransom. If they both hand over, the debt is voided because the slave leaves the pledgor's ownership due to a cause while in the mortgagee's hand and in his guarantee, so it becomes as if it died naturally, so the (debt) collection is confirmed. If one chooses handing over and the other refuses, it is not handed over. Because if the pledgor chooses handing over, he seeks to remove the pledgor's ownership without his consent, so he is prevented. And if the mortgagee chooses ransom, then the entire ransom is upon the mortgagee because the ransom is to ward off destruction from the slave and he revives his right by purifying it from the injury, like taking medicine to ward off destruction and the cost of the medicine is upon him because destruction is upon him; similarly the ransom. And it becomes like a usurped slave who injures: the injury is against the usurper because destruction is upon him; similarly this. And he does not recover the ransom from the pledgor because he has fulfilled an obligation upon him. And if the pledgor ransoms him, it is a payment of the debt if the ransom covers the entire debt, and it does not remain a pledge; if it covers part, then to that extent because he is not a volunteer in the ransom because it involves improving his ownership and extracting his right. And the slave is occupied with the injury, and the slave appears from the injury and revives his ownership, and the owner is not described as a volunteer in improving his ownership and reviving it. So he has fulfilled an obligation upon the mortgagee and he is compelled in it, so he has recourse against him. Like one who lends him [something] at the time of his pledging for his debt, then the lender pays the borrower's debt, he recovers from the borrower because he needs to free his ownership and purify it from the encumbrance of the pledge; similarly this. And if it [the slave] is destroyed in the mortgagee's hand after the pledgor ransomed him, the ransom is returned to the pledgor because the pledge becomes free of the debt by payment, because he has become a payer of his debt by the ransom. Some of our shaikhs said: he returns the thousand [that was] collected upon the destruction of the pledge, and what was found after the thousand is not attributed to the time of the pledge because the ransom has the effect of injury, and injury is a real action that does not admit of deficiency, and attribution does not appear in the case of actions that do not admit deficiency, so it becomes possible, so the collection by destruction is limited to the present. And if the collection by destruction is the latter of the two, then he returns what he collected last. And it becomes as if he pledged for the mahr or for the substitute of khul', then the mortgagee collected his debt, then the pledge is destroyed in his hand; he returns what he received because the payment of mahr is subject to revocation. And if the cause of the debt's obligation does not admit revocation, like marriage and khul', similarly this. All this is when ransom or handing over is chosen. If one chooses ransom and the other chooses handing over, then ransom is preferable because the one who chose handing over is capricious. As for the pledgor, because in handing over there is nullification of the mortgagee's right of detention, and his ownership of the slave does not cease, and his ownership of the ransom ceases to a substitute, for he recovers it from the mortgagee. So ransom is more beneficial to him than handing over. And the mortgagee by handing over intends to harm the pledgor without any benefit to himself, because his debt is remitted in both cases, and in handing over there is removal of the pledgor's ownership, while in ransom there is preservation of his ownership. So he is capricious, and no consideration is given to the choice of one who is capricious. All this is when the value of the pledge equals the debt or is less. If it is more, e.g., the slave's value is two thousand and the debt is one thousand, then if they choose ransom, the ransom is upon both halves because one half is guaranteed upon the mortgagee and the other half is a trust in his hand. So the guarantee is apportioned to the mortgagee and the trust to the pledgor, by considering the part as the whole. If the pledgor ransoms, he is a volunteer if the pledgor is present. If he is absent, he recovers half from him according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not recover in either case because he has paid a debt for another without his permission and he is not compelled in it, because the pledgor is forced to ransom the half when the mortgagee permits the ransom, and he does not improve his ownership nor revive his right because he has no ownership in the slave and no right in the half trust; unlike the pledgor. And he has [the argument] that the mortgagee has the right of detention and holding in the half trust if it is not guaranteed upon him, and he needs to revive his right and improve it, and in the ransom there is a revival of his right from one aspect, for he may reach his right by holding it, so he needs the ransom, so he is not described as a volunteer, so he needs the ransom. And if the pledgor ransoms while the mortgagee is absent, he is not a volunteer by agreement, and it leaves the pledge because he improves his own ownership and revives his right, and the owner in improving his ownership is not a volunteer, unless the mortgagee wishes to pay half the ransom. And if the pledgor hands him over, then the mortgagee has [the right to] ...
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on a detailed case not covered by the original author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It is taken from al-Mabsut by Imam al-Sarakhsi. The case: a pledged slave kills a free man by mistake (qatl khata'). The ruling examines the options of handing over the slave to the victim's family (daf') or paying ransom (fida') to free him. The analysis is divided based on the slave's value relative to the debt. (1) If value equals debt: Both pledgor and mortgagee must agree on handing over or ransom. If both hand over, the debt is cancelled. If one refuses, the other cannot force it. If ransom is chosen, it is entirely on the mortgagee because he benefits by preserving his right. If the pledgor ransoms, it counts as payment of the debt to the extent of the ransom, and he can recover from the mortgagee if necessary. If the slave dies after pledgor's ransom, the ransom is returned because the debt is considered paid. (2) If value exceeds debt: The ransom is split equally because half the value is under the mortgagee's guarantee (equivalent to debt) and half is a trust (amanah) with him. If the pledgor ransoms, the issue of volunteerism (tabarru') arises. Abu Hanifa says if the pledgor is absent, he can recover half from the mortgagee; if present, he is a volunteer. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say he cannot recover in either case because he is not compelled. The text gives the reasoning for each position. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing further scenarios.

Key Terms
الفداء (al-fida'): Ransom paid to free a slave from liability for homicide; here, it refers to paying the victim's family to avoid handing over the slave.
متعنت (muta'annit): Capricious or obstinate; one who chooses an option that harms the other party without benefit to himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why cannot the pledgor recover half the ransom from the mortgagee in either case?
A) Because the mortgagee is always absent.
B) Because the pledgor is paying a debt for another without permission and is not compelled.
C) Because the mortgagee's right of detention prevents it.
D) Because the slave is not destroyed.

Key Takeaway
When the pledged slave's value exceeds the debt, Abu Hanifa allows recovery of half the ransom if the mortgagee is absent; the Sahibayn never allow recovery, considering the pledgor a volunteer.

The Blind Pledge: Abu Hanifa vs Muhammad on Body Equivalence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on body equivalence in pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the status of a pledged slave who becomes blind after injury and explain the underlying legal principle of body equivalence.

Core Idea
The blind body stands in place of the healthy body for pledge purposes, preserving the full debt.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific scenario: a pledged slave blinds another slave, and the blind slave becomes the new pledge. The core question is whether the pledge covers the full original debt or only part of it. We will unpack the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, focusing on the legal concept of body equivalence (al-juththa al-ʿamyāʾ taqūmu maqām al-ṣaḥīḥ).

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Understand the facts: a person pledges a slave worth 1000. That slave pokes out the eyes of another slave worth 1000. The offending slave is handed over to the injured party, and the blind slave becomes the new pledged item.
Only one slave causes injury; the other becomes blind.
2
Identify the three positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the blind slave remains a pledge for the full 1000. Muhammad holds that the pledge is reduced proportionally to the blind slave's remaining value. Note the nuance in Abu Yusuf's view depending on the injured party's choice.
The dispute is not about validity but about the extent of coverage.
3
Trace the principle of body equivalence
Examine Abu Hanifa's foundational principle: the blind body legally stands in place of the healthy body (flesh and blood). Therefore, the injured party cannot both keep the body and claim damages; the blind body substitutes for the lost value.
This principle is key to understanding the different rulings.
4
Apply to Muhammad's and Abu Yusuf's views
Muhammad rejects full equivalence: the blind body is not a substitute for both body and eyes. He divides the offending slave's value between body and eyes. Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on the injured party's choice (keep body or hand it over).
Each position uses a different analogy or choice scenario.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa's view, the creditor's security remains intact for the full debt. Under Muhammad's view, the debt reduces, potentially leaving the creditor under-secured. The mufta bihi is likely Abu Hanifa's position.
This affects real-world lending and collateral management.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا رهن عبداً قيمته ألف عيني عبد قيمته ألف فدفع به وأخذ الأعمى فهو رهن بألف عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: يدفع العبد بجنايته والعبد المدفوع يقوم صحيحاً أو أعمى فيبطل من الرهن بقدره إن كان ثلثان فيبطل ثلثا الدين ويصير الأعمى رهناً بما بقي من الدين، وإن شاء الراهن سلمه للمرتهن بما بقي من الدين، وإن شاء أخذه بما بقي، وهذا بناء على أن عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى الجثة العمياء تقوم مقام الصحيح لحماً ودماً، وكذلك تقوم مقام القيمة لزوماً وحتماً حتى لا يكون صاحب الجثة العمياء أن يمسك الجثة ويضمن النقصان فيصير كأن التامة في يد المرتهن إلا أنه انتقصت قيمته بتراجع السعر فيبقى بجميع الدين . وعند محمد رحمه الله تعالى قيمة الجثة العمياء لا تقوم مقام الجثة والعينين جميعاً ولا تكون بدلاً عنهما حتى إن له أن يمسك الجثة ويرجع بقيمة النقصان فكذلك العبد الجاني يكون بعضه بإزاء الجثة وبعضه بإزاء العينين، فما كان بإزاء العينين فات لا إلى بدل، وما كان بإزاء فات إلى بدله فسقط ما كان بإزاء العينين ويبقى ما كان بإزاء الجثة. وعند أبي يوسف كذلك القيمة بإزاء الجثة والعينين متى اختار المفقوء عينه إمساك الجثة وتضمين النقصان، فأما إذا اختار دفع الجثة وأخذ الجاني فالجاني كله يكون بدلاً عن العينين لا عن الجثة لأن الجاني إنما وجب دفعه بسبب الجناية فيقوم مقام الفائت بالجناية والفائت بالجثة العينان لا الجثة، وكان كما لو فقأ عيناً واحدة وأخذ نصف قيمة المفقوء كان المأخوذ بدلاً عن الفائت فكذا إذا فقأ العينين إلا أن بدل العينين بدل جميع الرقبة كما في الحر والأصل أن توفر على المالك بدل ملكه فإن يزال المبدل يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك واحد وقد تعذر إزالة العين عن ملك المفقوأة لفواتها عن ملكه فجعلنا الجثة قائمة مقام العينين والمدفوع كان بإزاء العينين فصار الرهن فائتاً إلى خلف وإن كان أقل قيمة فيبقى بجميع الدين
If a person pledges a slave worth one thousand, and the slave pokes out the eyes of a slave worth one thousand, and he gives the (injured) slave in compensation and takes the blind (slave), then it is a pledge for one thousand according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: The (injuring) slave is handed over for his crime, and the handed-over slave is evaluated as healthy or blind, and accordingly part of the pledge is voided. If it is two-thirds, two-thirds of the debt are voided, and the blind slave becomes a pledge for the remaining debt. The pledgor may either surrender it to the pledgee for the remaining debt or take it for the remaining debt. This is based on: According to Abu Hanifa, the blind body stands in place of the healthy body in flesh and blood, and likewise stands in place of the value necessarily and inevitably, so that the owner of the blind body cannot retain the body and claim compensation for the deficiency; thus it becomes as if the complete body is in the hand of the pledgee, except that its value decreased due to price fluctuation, so it remains for the entire debt. According to Muhammad, the value of the blind body does not stand in place of both the body and the eyes, nor is it a substitute for them, so he may retain the body and claim compensation for the deficiency. Similarly, the offending slave is partly in exchange for the body and partly for the eyes; what was in exchange for the eyes is lost without replacement, and what was in exchange for the body is lost with its replacement, so the part for the eyes drops and the part for the body remains. According to Abu Yusuf, likewise, the value is in exchange for both body and eyes when the one whose eye was poked out chooses to retain the body and claim compensation for the deficiency. But if he chooses to hand over the body and take the offender, then the whole offender is a substitute for the eyes only, not for the body, because the offender is only due to be handed over due to the crime, so it stands in place of what was lost due to the crime, and what was lost due to the crime is the eyes, not the body. It is like if he poked out one eye and took half the value of the injured; the taken half is a substitute for what was lost. Similarly, if he pokes out both eyes, except that the substitute for both eyes is the substitute for the entire slave, as in the case of a free person. The principle is that the owner should have the substitute for his property; indeed, if the original is removed, the substitute and the original cannot be in one ownership, but since removal of the eyes from the ownership of the injured is impossible due to their loss from his ownership, we make the body stand in place of the eyes, and the handed-over (slave) was in exchange for the eyes, so the pledge is lost to a replacement, even if it is of lesser value, so it remains for the entire debt.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific scenario: a person pledges a slave worth 1000, and that slave pokes out the eyes of another slave worth 1000. The injured party is given the offending slave as compensation (or the offending slave is handed over), and the injured slave (now blind) becomes the new pledged item. The dispute centers on whether the blind slave (the original pledged item) continues to cover the entire original debt or only part of it. The passage first states the positions: According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the blind slave remains a pledge for the full 1000. According to Muhammad, the blind slave is evaluated as healthy or blind; if its healthy value was two-thirds of the original, then two-thirds of the debt becomes void, and the blind slave becomes a pledge only for the remaining one-third. The passage then explains the underlying reasoning. Abu Hanifa holds that the blind body is legally considered equivalent to the healthy body for compensation purposes; thus the injured party cannot both keep the body and claim damages—the blind body itself substitutes for the lost value, and the pledge remains intact for the full debt, with any decrease in value considered like a price fluctuation. Muhammad holds that the blind body is not equivalent to the combination of body and eyes; the injured party may keep the body and claim the deficiency. Therefore, part of the offending slave's value corresponds to the eyes (which are lost without replacement), and part to the body (which is replaced). Thus the pledge is reduced proportionally. Abu Yusuf's position is nuanced: if the injured party chooses to keep the blind body and claim deficiency, the value covers both; if he chooses to hand over the body and take the offending slave, then the offending slave substitutes only for the eyes, not the body. The passage uses analogical reasoning: it compares this to the case of poking out one eye, where the taken half-value is for the eye; here, the entire offending slave is for both eyes. The key legal principle is that the owner should receive the replacement for his property; since the eyes are irreplaceable, the body stands in for them, and the pledge remains with its replacement.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge or collateral; an item left as security for a debt.
الجثة العمياء (al-juththa al-ʿamyāʾ): The blind body; the physical body of a slave who has lost eyesight.
المفقوء عينه (al-mafqūʾ ʿaynah): The person whose eye has been poked out (the victim).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the status of the blind pledged slave after the offending slave is handed over?
A) It becomes void and the debt is cancelled.
B) It remains a pledge for the full original debt.
C) It remains a pledge for only half the debt.
D) The pledgor must pay the difference.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mainstream view (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) is that a pledged item remains fully securing the debt even if it loses value due to injury, based on the legal equivalence of the body.

Ruling on Pledged Slave Destroying Property
Ruling on sale of pledged slave for damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pledged slave destroys property and explain the disposition of the sale proceeds.

Core Idea
A pledged slave's destruction leads to his sale; surplus remains pledged.

In Hanafi pledge law, if a pledged slave causes damage to a third party's property, the slave is sold to compensate the victim. This ruling clarifies that any surplus from the sale remains part of the original pledge, serving as continued security for the debt. The principle of substitution (badal) ensures the integrity of the pledge arrangement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Harm
Recognize that the scenario involves a pledged slave (al-'abd al-marhun) who destroys property (atlafa mata'an) belonging to another person.
The slave is a pledge (rahn) securing a debt; the destruction creates a liability.
2
Determine the Remedy: Sale of the Slave
The slave is sold (yuba' fihi) – this sale is for the purpose of compensating the victim out of the proceeds.
The sale is a direct response to the tort committed by the slave.
3
Allocate the Sale Proceeds
First, the price is used to pay compensation to the victim. If anything remains (fa in baqiya shay'), that remainder is still a pledge (fa-huwa rahn).
It becomes a substitute (badal) for part of the original slave, analogous to compensation for a limb (arsh).
4
Determine the Final Beneficiary
Any remaining amount ultimately belongs to the pledgee (fa-huwa lil-murtahin) because the sale transfers the entire benefit to him.
The surplus is effectively a substitute for the pledgee's right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد الرهن أتلف متاعاً لرجل يباع فيه، ثمنه فإن بقي من شيء فهو رهن لأنه بدل بعض الرهن فيقوم مقام المبدل كأرش طرفه، فإن بقي شيء فهو للمرتهن لأنه بالبيع صار كلها لك في حقه فصار مستوفياً ومتملكاً له فيكون الثمن بدل ملكه فيكون له .
The pledged slave destroys property of a man; he is sold for it, and from his price, if anything remains, it is a pledge because it is a substitute for part of the pledge, so it stands in place of the substituted item like the compensation for one of his limbs. If anything remains, it belongs to the pledgee because by the sale it all becomes his in his right, so he becomes the recipient and owner of it; thus the price is a substitute for his property, so it is his.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage provides a brief ruling on a specific scenario: a pledged slave destroys someone's property. The rule: the slave is sold, and the proceeds are used to compensate the victim. If any surplus remains after paying the victim, that surplus remains as the original pledge (it substitutes for the destroyed property of the pledge? Actually 'بدل بعض الرهن' means substitute for part of the pledge, meaning it covers the lost value of the pledge? Wait: the text says 'فهو رهن لأنه بدل بعض الرهن' meaning the remaining amount is a pledge because it is a substitute for part of the pledge. This is due to the analogy with compensation for a limb (arsh). If any surplus remains after payment to the pledgee? Actually the sentence: 'فإن بقي شيء فهو للمرتهن' - if anything remains, it belongs to the pledgee. This seems contradictory: first it says 'فهو رهن' then 'فهو للمرتهن'. The likely interpretation: the slave is sold; from the sale price, the victim's compensation is paid first. If anything remains after that, it becomes part of the pledge (i.e., it stays as security), and eventually belongs to the pledgee because it substitutes for his right. The phrase 'صار كلها لك' is unclear, but it means the pledgee becomes entitled to the entire benefit of the sale. This is a straightforward ruling without proof, intended for memorization.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): Substitute or compensation; the sale price of the slave that replaces the lost property.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a limb or property; here, compensation for the destroyed property.

Liability from a Pledged Slave's Acts: The Well-Digging Scenarios
Detailed analysis of four well-digging scenarios with qiyas and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pledged slave's well-digging causes sequential injuries and distinguish between liability for human vs. property victims.

Core Idea
The order of falls and the type of victim determine liability and the master's options.

The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not address these scenarios, but Al-Mabsut provides a detailed treatment. The passage applies the principles of causation (tasabbub) and guarantee (daman) to a pledged slave who digs a well on a public road. The master's options differ based on whether the victim is a human or property, and the sequence of falls affects subsequent claims.

Guided Path
1
Map the four scenarios
Identify the four possible sequences of falls: animal then animal, human then human, human then animal, animal then human. Note that the slave digs while pledged, then the pledgor redeems the slave.
The scenarios are distinguished by order and type of victim.
2
Trace the master's options
Apply Muhammad's general rule from Al-Asl: for a crime against a human, the master chooses between handing over the slave or paying ransom (fidyah). For a crime against property, he chooses between selling the slave to pay the price or ransoming.
This distinction is key to understanding the subsequent rulings.
3
Apply the analogy
The passage uses qiyas on a usurped slave who digs a well. When the slave is sold or dies in the pledgee's hand, the pledgee is deemed to have received the debt, so the pledgor can reclaim the debt from the pledgee.
The ruling for the first scenario (animal falls) is derived from this analogy.
4
Analyze the order effect
If an animal falls first and then a human, the slave is sold for the animal, and the human's blood is wasted (sale cannot be rescinded). If a human falls first and then an animal, the guardian of the human gets the option of sale or ransom.
The order determines whether the later victim has a valid claim.

Source Text

The author did not address the issues of the pledge's injury by digging, but in Al-Mabsut: A man pledges a slave for one thousand, and the slave digs a well on the road while with the pledgee. Then the pledgor redeems the pledge and takes the slave. This has four scenarios: either an animal falls in, then another animal; or a person falls in, then another person; or a person falls in, then an animal. If an animal falls in and dies, its value being one thousand, the slave is sold for the debt unless the master ransoms him, because the slave destroyed the animal by digging. If a slave destroys someone's property, his master is told: either sell the slave or pay his debt. If he sells the slave for one thousand and the owner of the animal takes it, the pledgor then claims from the pledgee the debt he paid, because the slave was lost in the pledgee's guarantee, since he left the master's ownership due to a cause occurring in the pledgee's ownership; so it is considered as if he left his ownership by death in the hand of the pledgee, and he has already received his debt; so the pledgor claims from him what he collected by actual receipt. It becomes like the case of a usurped slave who dug a well on the road while in the hand of the usurper, then the usurper returned him to his master, and then an animal perished in the well; the ruling is as we described. Similarly here. If another animal worth one thousand falls into the well, it shares with the first animal's owner and takes half of what the first took, because he became the destroyer of both animals by the digging from the moment of becoming a cause, since his only action was digging, so digging was the cause of the destruction of both animals, so he became the destroyer of both together, and thus their values become a debt upon the slave. The master does not claim anything from the pledgor because his right is in the price of the slave and his earnings, and what the pledgor took from the pledgee is not the slave's price nor his earnings. If a person perishes in it, then the slave is handed over for him; the pledgor then claims from the pledgee what he paid of the debt, because the slave was lost in his hand, so he becomes as having received the debt from the time of the pledge, and he had already received it once before, so he must return one of the two debts. If another person perishes after the slave was handed over, then the second guardian shares with the first in the slave as we mentioned. If an animal falls in, so the slave is sold and its price is paid to its owner, then a person falls and dies, his blood is wasted. It would seem that the sale should be rescinded and then the slave handed over to the guardian for the crime, and then sold for the slave's debt. The answer is that rescinding the sale is useless, because if we rescind it we would need to repeat it a second time, which is an unnecessary engagement for the judge, and the judge does not engage in what is useless. If a person falls and dies, so the slave is handed over for the crime, then an animal falls in, the guardian of the slain is told: either sell the slave or pay the debt, because the two crimes are attributed to the time of digging, so they are as if they occurred together; so the slave is handed over to the guardian of the crime, and he is given the choice between selling and ransoming. Similarly here. It can be said: First, one should know that when a slave commits a crime, either his crime is against a human or against property of an animal or other, and the ruling differs. Muhammad said in Al-Asl: If a slave commits a crime against a human that necessitates monetary compensation, his master has the choice: either hand him over for it, or ransom him by paying the compensation. He differentiated between his crime against a human and his crime against property: in crime against a human, the master chooses between handing over and ransoming; in his crime against another's property, the master chooses between selling and paying the price, or ransoming. So in the example of digging a well on the road, if an animal falls and dies, and the master sells the slave and pays its price for the crime to the animal's owner, then another animal perishes, the owner of the second animal follows the owner of the first animal, because when the master sold and paid the price, he did what was obligatory and left the guarantee. When a person falls later, his blood is wasted because demand is impossible from the owner after he left the guarantee, and the price stands in the place of the slave's acquittal for the buyer. In the second issue, when he handed over the slave in person to the guardian of the first crime, then another person falls into the well, and the slave itself remains in the ownership of the first crime's guardian, and a new crime occurs due to the second fall and destruction due to his previous digging, and he had been handed over in person to the first. So his owner is addressed in the first crime with the original option of handing over or ransoming, and his statement is sound because the two crimes are attributed to the time of digging, etc. It can be answered: We do not concede that his blood is wasted, because of what we mentioned in Al-Mabsut regarding the crime of the slave digging: if a slave dug a well on the road, then was emancipated, and then fell into it...

Commentary
This long passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, quoting Al-Mabsut, presents a detailed treatment of cases where a pledged slave digs a well on a public road, leading to various injuries. The passage opens by noting that the author (presumably the commentator) did not address such cases, but Al-Mabsut did. Al-Mabsut presents four scenarios involving the order of falls (animal/animal, human/human, human/animal, animal/human). The passage then discusses each scenario with its specific ruling, often involving the sale of the slave, payment of compensation, and the rights of the pledgor and pledgee. The reasoning relies on analogies (qiyas) and the principle of attribution of cause (tasabbub). For example, when the first fall is an animal, the slave is sold, and if the pledgor pays the debt, he can claim from the pledgee because the slave was lost in the pledgee's guarantee. This is analogized to the case of a usurped slave who digs a well. When two animals fall, liability is shared. If a human falls, the slave is handed over, and the pledgor can claim from the pledgee. If a human falls after the slave has been handed over for a previous crime, the second guardian shares in the slave. The passage also discusses the case where a human falls after the slave was sold for an animal, ruling that the human's blood is wasted (no claim) because the sale cannot be rescinded. The passage then brings a statement from Muhammad in Al-Asl distinguishing between crimes against humans and against property, where the master's options differ. For crimes against humans, the master can choose to hand over the slave or ransom; for property, he can choose to sell and pay the price or ransom. This distinction is applied to the well-digging scenarios. The passage ends with a possible objection and a response referencing another case of emancipation, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
جناية (jināya): A crime or injury causing harm to another's person or property.
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee or liability; here, the pledgee's guarantee over the pledged slave.
فداء (fidāʾ): Ransom; payment to free the slave from liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Injury by Pawn to Third Party and Its Attribution
States ruling when pawn injures a third party and discusses the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding attribution of injury by a pawn to the mortgagee, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) to determine liability.

Core Idea
Injury by a pawn is attributed to the pawnor, not the mortgagee—unless the pawn's value exceeds the debt.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a pawn injures a third party, is the injury considered against the mortgagee? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples diverge on this, each drawing analogies from a usurped slave. Understanding their reasoning clarifies the liability of the mortgagee and the effect on the debt.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
When a pawn injures a stranger, if the pawn is given as compensation the debt is discharged; if not, it remains pawned. This is agreed upon by all.
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
2
Identify the contested issue
The dispute is whether the injury is considered against the mortgagee (i.e., does the mortgagee become liable for compensation?).
3
Present Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that injury by a pawn is not considered against the mortgagee. He analogizes to a usurped slave: injury by a usurped slave is not attributed to the usurper because the slave is not owned by him.
Qiyas to al-'abd al-maghsub.
4
Present the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the injury against the mortgagee. They analogize to the custody and benefit: the mortgagee has possession and enjoys the pawn's service, so injuries should be attributed to him.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf and exception
The practical consequence: if considered against mortgagee, he must pay compensation; if not, the pawn is liable and the debt may remain. A narration from Abu Hanifa qualifies: if pawn value exceeds debt, the excess is a trust, and injury is considered proportionally against mortgagee regarding that excess.
This exception is analogous to a deposited slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الرهن باب التصرف للرهن والجناية عليه وجنايته على غيره جنى على الأجنبي فدفع بها سقط الدين، وإن لم يدفع بالجناية فهو رهن على حاله. ولأبي حنيفة أن هذه الجناية لو اعتبرناها للمرتهن كان عليه التطهير من الجناية لأنها حصلت في ضمانه فلا تفيد وجوب الضمان مع وجوب التخليص عليه وهذا الاختلاف نظير الاختلاف في العبد المغصوب فإن جنايته على الغاصب لا تعتبر عنده وعندهما تعتبر. وما ذكرا من الفائدة غير ظاهر لأن أخذ العبد بالجناية لا يكون إلا باختيار المالك. وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة إذا كانت قيمة الرهن أكثر من الدين فإن كانت جنايته على المرتهن معتبرة بحسابها لأن الزائد أمانة فصار كجناية العبد المودع . قيد بقوله «عليهما» لأن جنايته على أولادهما معتبرة فلو جنى الرهن على ابن الراهن أو على ابن المرتهن فهي معتبرة في الصحيح حتى يدفع بها أو يفيد، وإن كانت على المال فيباع كما إذا جنى على الأجنبي إذ هو أجنبي كسائر الأملاك.
Book of Pawn, Chapter on Disposition of the Pawn and Injury upon It and Its Injury upon Others. If [the pawn] injures a stranger and is paid with it, the debt is discharged; if not paid with the injury, it remains a pawn in its state. According to Abu Hanifa, if we consider this injury as against the mortgagee, then he would be obligated to clear the injury because it occurred in his guarantee, so it does not necessitate liability along with the obligation of release upon him. This difference is analogous to the difference regarding a usurped slave: his injury against the usurper is not considered according to him (Abu Hanifa), but is considered according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). What they mention as benefit is not apparent because taking the slave for injury only occurs with the owner's choice. In a narration from Abu Hanifa: if the value of the pawn exceeds the debt, then if its injury is against the mortgagee, it is considered proportionally because the excess is a trust, so it becomes like the injury of a deposited slave. He restricted with 'against them' because injury against their children is considered. If the pawn injures the son of the pawnor or the son of the mortgagee, it is considered in the correct opinion, so that he pays with it or redeems. If the injury is against property, it is sold as if it injured a stranger, since he is a stranger like other properties.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling when a pawn (rahn) injures a third party (ajnabi). The author begins by stating the basic rule: if the pawn injures someone and the pawn itself is given as compensation (i.e., turned over to the victim), the debt secured by the pawn is discharged. If the pawn is not given up for the injury, it remains pawned. This is a Zahir al-Riwayah position. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Abu Hanifa holds that such an injury by the pawn should not be considered as an injury against the mortgagee (murtahin). If it were, the mortgagee would be responsible for clearing the pawn from the injury (i.e., paying compensation) since the pawn is in his custody. This would create a conflict: he would be liable for the injury while also being obligated to preserve the pawn for the debt. Therefore, the injury is considered against the pawnor (rahin) or against the pawn itself, not against the mortgagee. The Sahibayn disagree and consider it against the mortgagee. Abu Hanifa draws an analogy (qiyas) to a usurped slave (al-'abd al-maghsub): according to him, if a usurped slave injures someone, the injury is not attributed to the usurper (ghasib) because the slave does not belong to him; but the Sahibayn attribute it to the usurper. The text notes a narration from Abu Hanifa that if the pawn's value exceeds the debt, then the excess portion is a trust (amanah) in the mortgagee's hands, and in that case injury by the pawn on the mortgagee is considered proportionally, analogous to a deposited slave. The passage concludes by clarifying the phrase 'against them' in the text: injury by the pawn against the children of the pawnor or mortgagee is considered valid (mu'tabara) such that the pawn can be used to pay or redeem the debt. However, if the injury is against property (mal) rather than a person, the pawn is sold as if it injured a stranger.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pawn or mortgage given as security for a debt; the property remains owned by the pawnor but in custody of the mortgagee.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the person who receives the pawn as security for a debt.
الراهن (al-rahin): The pawnor, the person who gives the pawn as security.
جناية (jinaayah): An injurious act causing harm to person or property, leading to liability.
العبد المغصوب (al-'abd al-maghsub): A slave who is wrongfully taken (usurped) from the owner; his injuries are attributed to the usurper according to Sahibayn, but not according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a pawn injures a stranger and the pawn is not given as compensation, what happens to the pawn?
A) The pawn becomes free and the debt is discharged.
B) The pawn remains pawned as it was.
C) The mortgagee must pay compensation.
D) The pawnor loses ownership.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, injury by a pawn is attributed to the pawn, not the mortgagee, but when the pawn's value exceeds the debt, the excess becomes a trust and injury on the mortgagee is considered proportionally.

Injuries Between Pawns: Mashghul and Farigh
Sets forth the rule classifying injuries between pawns as hadar or mu'tabara based on mashghul/farigh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify injuries between pawns as hadar or mu'tabara and explain the reasoning (ta'lil) based on the concepts of mashghul and farigh.

Core Idea
Injury between pawns is annulled if both parts are encumbered; if at least one is unencumbered, compensation transfers the debt.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rule for injuries between two pawns held under the same debt. The distinction between the encumbered (mashghul) and unencumbered (farigh) parts determines whether the injury is annulled or considered. Understanding this protects the mortgagee's security interest and clarifies when compensation is due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of each pawn
Each pawn has a part equal to the debt (mashghul) and any excess value (farigh). Determine the mashghul and farigh portions based on the debt amount and the pawn's value.
If the pawn's value is less than or equal to the debt, the entire pawn is mashghul.
2
State the rule for each combination
There are four possible injury combinations: mashghul on mashghul, mashghul on farigh, farigh on farigh, and farigh on mashghul. For each, state whether it is hadar (annulled) or mu'tabara (considered).
The text gives the rule: mashghul on mashghul is hadar but debt drops; farigh on farigh is hadar; the other two are mu'tabara.
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The injury is considered only if it benefits the mortgagee's right of lien (habs). The only case that truly benefits is farigh on mashghul, because the debt transfers to the injurer, preserving the total security. In other cases, no new benefit accrues.
The passage states that considering the injury for the mortgagee's right only benefits in farigh on mashghul.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Take two pawns with given values and a debt. Identify the mashghul/farigh of each, then apply the rule to determine the outcome of an injury.
Example: Pawn A ($300) and Pawn B ($100) with $200 debt. A: mashghul=$200, farigh=$100; B: entirely mashghul. If farigh of A damages B, it is farigh on mashghul, mu'tabara, and the $200 debt transfers to A's farigh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ولم يتعرض المؤلف لبقية الجناية التي تكون هدر أو الجناية بعض الرهن على بعض قال في المبسوط : أصله أن جناية المشغول على المشغول هدر لكن يسقط الدين في المجني بقدره وجناية المشغول على الفارغ والفارغ على الفارغ وجناية الفارغ على المشغول معتبرة وينتقل ما في المشغول من الدين إلى الفارغ فيصير رهناً مكانه لأن الجناية إنما تعتبر لحق المرتهن في المجني عليه لا لحق الراهن لأن كلاهما ملكه واعتبار الجناية لحق المرتهن لا يفيد إلا في جناية الفارغ على المشغول لأنها إنما تعتبر ليشتغل الجاني بما كان من المجني عليه وهو الحبس، وهذا ثابت قبل الجناية فإن الجاني كان محبوساً بالدين الذي كان المجني عليه محبوساً به، ولهذا جناية الفارغ على الفارغ هدر لأنه لا شغل فيها بحق الحبس. وإذا لم يفد اعتبارها صار كأنه فات بآفة سماوية فإن جناية الفارغ على المشغول تفيد لأن الدين تحول إليه من المجني عليه فقام مقامه.
This, and the author did not address the rest of the injury that is annulled or injury of some pawn on another. He said in al-Mabsut: Its principle is that injury of the mashghul (occupied/encumbered) on the mashghul is annulled, but the debt drops from the injured party to its extent. The injury of the mashghul on the farigh (unencumbered), and the farigh on the farigh, and the injury of the farigh on the mashghul are considered, and what is of debt in the mashghul transfers to the farigh, so it becomes a pawn in its place. This is because injury is only considered for the right of the mortgagee in the injured party, not for the right of the pawnor, because both belong to him. Considering the injury for the mortgagee's right only benefits in the case of injury of the farigh on the mashghul, because it is only considered so that the injurer becomes occupied with what was in the victim, namely the lien (habs). This was established before the injury; the injurer was already held by the debt by which the victim was held. Therefore, injury of the farigh on the farigh is annulled, because there is no occupation by right of lien in it. And if its consideration does not benefit, it becomes as if it perished by a heavenly calamity. Thus injury of the farigh on the mashghul benefits because the debt transfers from the victim to the injurer, so it takes its place.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a principle from Al-Mabsut regarding injuries between two pawns held under the same debt. The key distinction is between mashghul (encumbered) and farigh (unencumbered) portions of the pawns. Each pawn has a part equal to the debt (mashghul) and any excess (farigh). The rule is: if a mashghul part injures another mashghul part, the injury is annulled (hadar), meaning no compensation is due and the debt on the victim is reduced proportionally. If a mashghul injures a farigh, a farigh injures a farigh, or a farigh injures a mashghul, the injury is considered (mu'tabara): compensation is due, and any debt on the victim transfers to the injurer. However, the only case that actually benefits the mortgagee is when a farigh injures a mashghul, because then the injurer becomes encumbered with the debt that was on the victim, preserving the security. In other cases, no new benefit accrues to the mortgagee. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the mortgagee's right is only in the lien (habs) on the pawn; when a farigh injures a mashghul, the injurer (who was free) becomes subject to the victim's lien, maintaining the total security. If a farigh injures another farigh, no lien is involved, so it's as if the loss was due to an act of God.

Key Terms
مشغول (mashghul): The portion of a pawn that is encumbered by the debt; equivalent to the amount of the debt relative to the pawn's value.
فارغ (farigh): The unencumbered portion of a pawn that exceeds the debt; it belongs to the pawnor but is in the mortgagee's custody as a trust.
هدر (hadar): Legally null; no compensation is due and no legal effect.
حَبس (habs): The right to retain the pawn as security; the lien that the mortgagee holds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which type of injury between pawns is considered (mu'tabara) and benefits the mortgagee?
A) Mashghul on mashghul
B) Mashghul on farigh
C) Farigh on farigh
D) Farigh on mashghul

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule on pawn injuries uses the mashghul/farigh distinction to determine whether compensation is due, based on the mortgagee's right of lien.

Injuries Between Pawns: Animals vs. Slaves
Applies the principle of injuries between pawns to specific cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rule for injury between two animal pawns and EXPLAIN how debt transfers in cases of killing or eye-gouging between slave pawns.

Core Idea
Animal pawn injuries are like natural death; slave pawn injuries transfer debt proportionally.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq applies the general principle of pawn injuries to two concrete examples: animals and slaves. The key distinction is the hadith 'The wound of a dumb animal is without liability' (jubar), which treats animal injuries as accidental. For slaves, liability exists, so the killer replaces the victim in the pawn arrangement. The passage then works through detailed calculations for killing and partial injury (eye-gouging), demonstrating how the debt is reallocated between the pawns based on whether they are half-occupied (mashghul) or half-free (farigh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for injuries
First, note that the passage introduces three chapters: injury upon the pawn, injury of the pawn's offspring, and injury of a borrowed pawn. Then it contrasts two specific cases: two animal pawns (dabbatayn) and two slave pawns (abdayn).
2
Apply the hadith to animal pawns
For animals, the hadith 'جرح العجماء جبار' (the wound of a dumb animal is without liability) means one animal destroying another is like natural death. Therefore, the debt on the destroyed animal is simply reduced from the total debt; no compensation is owed.
The debt is reduced proportionally to the value of the destroyed animal.
3
Understand the distinction for slave pawns
For slaves, injury is considered because retaliation or compensation is possible. The killer slave takes the place of the victim in terms of encumbrance; the victim's debt transfers to the killer.
This is the main difference: liability exists for slaves but not for animals.
4
Trace the killing scenario calculation
Two slaves pawned in one transaction for 1000, each worth 1000. Each slave is half occupied (mashghul = 500) and half free (farigh = 500). When one kills the other, the freed portions perish without compensation; only the occupied portions remain encumbered. The text states the remaining pawn is 950 (then 750, possibly a textual error), but the principle is that the killer's pawn now bears the combined debt.
The calculations are intricate; focus on the method of dividing into occupied and free halves.
5
Trace the eye-gouging scenario calculation
Eye-gouging destroys half the slave's value. Using the same half-occupied half-free model, the passage works through several subcases: one slave gouges the other, both gouge each other successively, or both simultaneously. The debt shifts proportionally, and the pawns cannot be redeemed separately.
The final sentence is garbled, but the overall rule is that the currency of the pawn is unified.

If he pawns two animals and one destroys the other, the debt is reduced proportionally, unlike if the pawn were two slaves and one kills the other: the debt of the slain transfers to the killer because the injury of a dumb animal is without liability (jubar) due to his saying, peace be upon him, 'The wound of a dumb animal is without liability.' Commentary
This passage provides practical applications (furu') of the principle of injuries between pawns. The author first outlines three chapters: injury upon the pawn, injury of the pawn's offspring, and injury of a borrowed pawn. Then he gives two examples. First, if two animals (dabbatayn) are pawned and one injures the other fatally, the debt is reduced proportionally to the value of the destroyed animal. This is because the hadith 'The wound of a dumb animal is without liability' (jinaayat al-'ajma' jubar) means such injury is like natural death; no compensation is due, but the security is reduced. Second, if two slave pawns are involved, the rules differ: injury between slaves is considered such that retaliation, payment, or ransom may be required. The killer slave takes the place of the victim in terms of encumbrance. The author then gives a detailed scenario: two slaves pawned in one transaction for 1000 (each worth 1000). Each slave has half mashghul (500) and half farigh (500). If one kills the other, the debt for the remaining pawn is calculated as 950 (the text says 950 but then says 750? It says 'فصار كأنه رهن بسبعمائة وخمسين' (750). This seems contradictory; likely the text has a copying error. The calculation: the victim's farigh half perishes by jinaayat al-farigh 'ala al-mashghul and farigh 'ala farigh (both hadar), and the mashghul half perishes by mashghul 'ala mashghul (hadar). So no compensation, but the debt that was on the victim (500) is lost from the security. The killer's half mashghul remains, but now the killer is the only pawn; his total debt encumbrance becomes 500 (his own) plus the victim's debt? The text says 'فاق الباقي رهن بتسعمائة وخمسين' (950) then 'رهن بسبعمائة وخمسين' (750). This may be corruption. The correct calculation: the killer's pawn now bears both debts, so it should be 1000? But since half is farigh, only the mashghul half (500) is initially encumbered. After killing, the victim's debt transfers to the killer, but only to the extent that the killer's farigh portion can absorb. Since the killer's farigh is 500, the victim's debt of 500 transfers there, making the killer fully encumbered (1000). The remaining value of the killer is still 1000, so it becomes pawn for 1000? The text's numbers need careful re-examination. Then the author moves to eye-gouging, which is a partial injury. Since an eye is considered half of a human's value, the calculation becomes intricate. The text calculates: 'Eye of a human is half' – meaning the value of an eye is half the slave's value. The debtor and pawn are interlinked. For a pawned slave worth 1000, with 500 debt (half mashghul, half farigh), gouging an eye destroys worth 500. The calculations show how the debt shifts. The overall principle is that the pawn cannot be redeemed except together because the multiple pawns are considered one security. The final line is garbled, but the gist is that after various pairwise gougings, the remaining debt and encumbrance are apportioned.

Key Terms
العجماء (al-'ajma'): A dumb animal (beast) that cannot speak; its injury is without liability (jubar) according to the hadith.
جبار (jubar): Without liability; the injury is considered an act of God or natural event; no compensation is due.
فقأ العين (faq' al-'ayn): Gouging out the eye; a specific type of injury that destroys half the value of a human slave.
صفقة (safqah): Transaction; here, either one contract (safqah wahidah) or separate contracts (safqatayn) for the two pawns.
